Talaq — Divorce
beginner

When 'Except One' Is Said from Three Divorces: The Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on divorce count when 'except one' is stated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of a connected exception (istithna) in a divorce pronouncement and apply the Hanafi position to determine the number of divorces that occur.

Core Idea
A valid, connected exception subtracts from the total count — 'three except one' leaves two divorces.

This passage addresses a common scenario in divorce pronouncements: the husband says 'You are divorced three except one.' Does one divorce occur, or two? The Hanafi school, relying on a hadith about Allah's names, treats the exception as valid and subtractive, resulting in two divorces. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly counting divorces and determining the possibility of reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the pronouncement and the exception
The husband said 'You are divorced three except one.' The key is that 'three' is the intended number, and 'except one' is the exception. The exception must be connected (muttasil) to be valid.
In Hanafi law, a disconnected exception (e.g., a pause) invalidates the exception, and three divorces would occur.
2
Determine the intended number after subtraction
The exception 'except one' subtracts one from the intended three. The result is two divorces. This is a straightforward arithmetic operation based on linguistic usage.
3
Locate the dalil (evidence)
The author cites a hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: 'Indeed Allah has ninety-nine names, one hundred except one.' This shows that exception in numbers subtracts from the total, leaving the remainder.
The hadith is sahih and serves as a linguistic precedent for how exception functions.
4
Apply analogical reasoning (qiyas)
Just as 'one hundred except one' means ninety-nine, 'three except one' means two. The exception removes the specified number from the total. The Hanafi school applies this linguistic rule analogically to divorce pronouncements.
The dalala (indication) is zanni (probable), but the ruling is well-established in the school.
5
State the ruling
Therefore, when a husband says 'You are divorced three except one,' two divorces occur, assuming the exception is connected and valid.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان قال لها انت طالق ثلثا الا واحدة طلقت ثنتين [۱۹۹۷] (۸۳) اگر بیوی سے کہا تم کو طلاق ہے تین مگر ایک تو طلاق واقع ہوگی دو۔ تین طلاق میں سے ایک کو استثناء کر کے ساقط کر دیا تو دو طلاقیں رہیں اس لئے دو طلاقیں ہی واقع ہوں گی (۲) حدیث میں ایسا استثناء ہے۔عن ابي هريرة ان رسول الله قال ان لله تسعة وتسعين اسما مائة الا واحدا من احصاها دخل الجنة (ج) ( بخارى شریف ، باب ان اللہ مائکہ اسم الا واحدة ص ۱۰۹۹ نمبر ۷۳۹۲، کتاب التوحیدر مسلم شریف ، باب فی اسماء الله تعالى وفضل من احصا صاص ۱۳۴۲ نمبر ۲۶۷۷) اس حدیث میں سو میں سے ایک کو استثناء کیا جس کی بنا پر بناوے نام باقی رہے۔
And if he said to her, 'You are divorced three except one,' she is divorced two. [1997] (83) If the husband said to his wife, 'You are divorced three except one,' two divorces occur. Because from the three divorces, one is excluded and dropped, so two remain; hence two divorces occur. (2) There is such an exception in a hadith: From Abu Huraira that the Messenger of Allah said, 'Indeed Allah has ninety-nine names, one hundred except one; whoever enumerates them will enter Paradise.' (c) (Bukhari, chapter on Allah having one hundred names except one, p. 1099, no. 7392, and Muslim, chapter on the names of Allah, p. 1342, no. 2677) In this hadith, one is excluded from one hundred, and ninety-nine remain.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof. The passage states that if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced three except one,' three divorces are intended, but the exception of one excludes it, leaving two. Therefore, two divorces occur. The proof is from a hadith where the Prophet said Allah has ninety-nine names, 'one hundred except one,' showing that the exception subtracts from the total. Similarly, here the exception subtracts one from three, resulting in two.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna): Exception; a linguistic exclusion of something from a general statement, here in the context of divorce count.
طلاق (talaq): Divorce; the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced three except one,' how many divorces occur?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
A connected exception from a number of divorces subtracts that number, so 'three except one' results in two divorces.

Marriage Dissolves When Spouse Becomes Owner
Ruling on dissolution of marriage upon ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage dissolution upon ownership of a spouse and apply it to the four scenarios involving a share of ownership.

Core Idea
Ownership of any share of a spouse immediately dissolves the marriage, because equality is incompatible with ownership.

The Hanafi school holds that if a spouse acquires ownership of the other spouse or even a share, the marriage is automatically dissolved. This ruling is based on a well-known athar from Ali (RA) and is one of the cases where ownership and nikah cannot coexist. In this lesson, you will learn the ruling, its proof, and the practical scenarios it covers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the Hanafi position: marriage dissolves immediately (furqa) when a husband owns his wife or a share, or when a wife owns her husband or a share.
This is a fasikh (automatic dissolution), not a talaq.
2
Examine the Scenarios
The source lists four cases: free husband marries slave woman then buys/inherits a share; free wife marries slave then buys/inherits a share. All result in dissolution.
Only one share (shiqs) suffices.
3
Analyze the Proof (Dalil)
The primary proof is an athar from Ali (RA) where a woman inherited a share of her husband; Ali ruled she can dispose of him as a slave, indicating marriage is broken.
Similar athar from Umar (RA) also cited.
4
Understand the Legal Reasoning
The rationale: marriage requires equality between spouses, but ownership creates a superior–inferior relationship incompatible with that equality.
Analogy (qiyas) to a master owning a slave.
5
Note the Inter-Madhhab Difference
The Shafi‘i school holds that only the husband’s ownership of the wife dissolves the marriage; the reverse does not necessarily dissolve it.
Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text

[1999] (85) And if the husband owns his wife or a share of her, or the wife owns her husband or a share of him, the separation occurs between them. (85) If the husband becomes owner of his wife or a share of her, or the wife becomes owner of her husband or a share of him, then separation occurs between them. Footnote (a): The Prophet said: Whoever says to his wife 'You are divorced if Allah wills,' or to his slave 'You are free if Allah wills,' or 'I have to walk to the House of Allah if Allah wills,' then nothing is upon him. (b) Thawri said: A man swore by divorce that he would not speak to so-and-so for a month, then afterwards said 'except if I wish.' He said: If the speech is continuous, he has the exception; if he cut it off and was silent then made the exception afterwards, then no exception for him. (c) The Prophet said: Allah has ninety-nine names, one hundred except one; whoever enumerates them will enter Paradise. [Page 128] And if the husband owns his wife or a share of her, or the wife owns her husband or a share of him, the separation occurs between them. The husband was free and was married to a slave woman; later he purchased her or inherited her, so he became owner of a share of her. Or the wife was free and married a slave; later the wife purchased the husband or his share, so she became owner of the husband or his share. In these four cases, the marriage is broken. The rights of wife and husband are equal, but the owner and owned have great disparity; therefore, upon becoming owner, the marriage is broken. (2) There is an athar: From Ali that a woman inherited a share from her husband, so that was brought to Ali. He asked, 'Did you have intercourse with her?' He said no. Ali said, 'If you had intercourse, I would have stoned you with stones.' Then he said, 'He is your slave; if you wish, sell him; if you wish, gift him; if you wish, free him and marry him.' (a) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter on marriage and ownership of right hand cannot combine, vol. 7, p. 207, no. 13737). From this athar it is known that the marriage dissolves. There is also a similar athar from Umar (Sunan al-Bayhaqi vol. 7 p. 207 no. 13736). Footnote (a): Transmitted from Ali: A woman inherited a share from her husband, so this case was brought to Ali. He asked, 'Did you have intercourse with her?' He said no. Ali said, 'If you had intercourse, I would have stoned you.' Then he said, 'He is your slave; if you wish, sell him, or gift him, or free him and marry him.'
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proof: if a spouse becomes the owner of the other spouse or a share thereof, the marriage is immediately dissolved. The commentary explains four scenarios: (1) free husband marries a slave woman, then later buys her or inherits a share; (2) free wife marries a slave, then later buys him or his share. In all these, the marriage breaks because the relationship of ownership is incompatible with marital equality. The proof is an athar from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) where a woman inherited a share of her husband. Ali ruled that the marriage is dissolved and she can dispose of him as a slave. This shows the separation occurs upon ownership.

Key Terms
شقص (shiqs): A share or portion; part ownership in the spouse.
فرقة (furqa): Separation; dissolution of the marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a free woman marries a slave and later purchases half of him?
A) The marriage continues because she only owns half.
B) The marriage dissolves immediately.
C) The marriage is suspended until she owns the other half.
D) She must divorce him before the marriage can continue.

Key Takeaway
When a spouse acquires ownership (even partial) of the other, the marriage is automatically dissolved in the Hanafi school, based on the athar from Ali (RA).

Right of Return After Revocable Divorce (Raj'ah)
Ruling on husband's right to take back wife during 'iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of raj'ah, identify its conditions, and cite the Quranic and hadith proofs that establish it.

Core Idea
A husband may take back his wife during her iddah after one or two revocable divorces, without her consent.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on raj'ah — the husband's right to resume marital relations during the iddah after a revocable divorce. The passage provides scriptural evidence from the Quran and hadith, establishing that this is a unilateral right of the husband. Understanding the conditions and proofs of raj'ah is essential for correctly applying family law rulings in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is raj'ah? It is the husband taking his wife back during the iddah. The ruling is mubah (permissible).
Note that it is not obligatory but recommended if reconciliation is sought.
2
Locate the primary proof
Find the Quranic verses cited: 2:228 'their husbands are more entitled to take them back' and 2:229 'divorce is twice; then retain honorably'.
The phrase 'ahaqqu' (more entitled) indicates the husband's right.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does each proof specifically support the ruling? The word 'retain' in the verse is interpreted as raj'ah.
The hadith from Bukhari also commands the husband to take his wife back.
4
Identify additional proofs
The passage also cites Quran 65:2 and an effect from Ibn 'Abbas. These reinforce that raj'ah is only possible during the iddah.
After the iddah expires, raj'ah is not allowed.
5
Extract the conditions
The divorce must be revocable (one or two, not three), and the action must take place within the iddah. The wife's consent is not required.
Expiry of iddah or irrevocable divorce destroys the possibility of raj'ah.

Source Text

شوہر نے بیوی کو ایک طلاق رجعی یا دو طلاق رجعی دی۔ اب وہ عدت کے اندر اندر عورت سے رجعت کرنا چاہے تو کر سکتا ہے۔ اس رجعت پر عورت راضی ہو یا نہ ہو۔
ایک یا دو طلاقیں رجعی دی ہو تو اس پر رجعت کر سکتا ہے اس کی دلیل اوپر کی آیت الطلاق مرتان فامساک بمعروف او تسریح باحسان ہے۔ جس میں ہے کہ دو طلاقیں دی ہو تو معروف کے ساتھ روک سکتا ہے۔ اور عدت کے اندراندر رجعت کر سکتا ہے اس کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے۔ فاذا بلغن اجلھن فامسكوهن بمعروف او فارقوهن بمعروف واشهدوا ذوى عدل منكم (د) (آیت ۲ سورة الطلاق ۶۵) اس آیت میں ہے کہ اجل پر یعنی مدت پر پہنچ جائے یعنی عدت ختم ہونے کے قریب پہنچ جائے تو دو اختیار ہیں۔ ایک روک لینا اور دوسرا چھوڑ دینا۔ اس لئے عدت ختم ہو جائے تو اب رجعت نہیں کر سکتا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس وعن مرة عن عبد الله وعن اناس من اصحاب رسول الله الا الله فذكر التفسير الى قوله الطلاق مرتان قال هو الميقات الذي يكون عليها فيه الرجعة فاذا طلق واحدة أو ثنتين فاما ان يمسك ويراجع بمعروف واما يسكت عنها حتى تنقضى عدتها فتكون احق بنفسها (ه) (سن الیتی ، کتاب الرجعة ج سابع ص ۲۰۱، نمبر ۱۵۱۵۰ر مصنف ابن ابی شیته ۱۵۱، ما قالوافی قولہ الطلاق مرتان
حاشیہ : (ه) طلاق دو مرتبہ ہیں، فرمایا وہ وقت ہے جس میں رجعت ہو سکتی ہے۔ پس جب طلاق دے ایک یادو ( باقی اگلے صفحہ پر )
Chapter of Return (Raj'ah). Chapter of Return. 1970 (a) If a man divorces his wife one revocable divorce or two revocable divorces, he may take her back within her 'iddah. (Chapter of Return) Necessary Note: When a husband gives his wife one or two revocable divorces and takes her back within the 'iddah, this is called 'raj'ah'. He cannot take her back after an irrevocable divorce. The proof is the verse: 'And their husbands are more entitled to take them back during that [period] if they wish for reconciliation' (Quran 2:228). Another verse: 'Divorce is twice; then retain them honorably or release them kindly' (Quran 2:229). In this verse 'retain them honorably' means to take her back (raj'ah). (3) In the hadith: I heard Ibn 'Umar say: Ibn 'Umar divorced his wife while she was menstruating, so 'Umar mentioned it to the Prophet (ﷺ), who said: 'He should take her back.' (Bukhari, Chapter on divorce of menstruating woman, p. 790 no. 5252) This hadith commands raj'ah, thus establishing it. (1970) (1) If the husband gave his wife one revocable divorce or two revocable divorces, he has the option to take her back during the 'iddah, whether the woman is pleased or not. The husband gave one revocable divorce or two revocable divorces. Now he wishes to take her back during the 'iddah, he may do so, whether the woman is pleased with this return or not. If he gave one or two revocable divorces, he may take her back. Its proof is the above verse 'Divorce is twice; then retain them honorably or release them kindly', which says that when two divorces are given, he may retain honorably. And that he may take her back within the 'iddah, its proof is the verse: 'When they have nearly reached their term, then retain them honorably or part with them honorably, and call to witness two just persons among you' (Quran 65:2). In this verse, when they reach the end of the term, i.e., near the expiry of the 'iddah, there are two options: to retain or to release. Therefore, when the 'iddah expires, he cannot take her back. (2) There is an effect from Ibn 'Abbas and from Murrah from 'Abdullah and from a group of companions of the Messenger of Allah: He mentioned the interpretation up to His saying 'Divorce is twice'. He said: It is the time during which raj'ah is possible. So when he divorces once or twice, either he may retain and take back kindly, or he may remain silent until her 'iddah expires and then she becomes free to herself. (h) (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Book of Raj'ah, vol 7, p. 201, no. 15150 and Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah 151, what they said about the verse 'Divorce is twice') Footnote (h): 'Divorce is twice', he said: it is the time during which raj'ah is possible. So when he divorces once or two (rest on next page).
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Chapter of Return (Raj'ah). It states that a husband who has given one or two revocable divorces may take his wife back during the 'iddah, regardless of her consent. The proofs are from Quran (2:228, 2:229, 65:2) and hadith (Bukhari). The ruling is that raj'ah is permissible, not obligatory. The text emphasizes that after an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) or after the 'iddah expires, raj'ah is no longer possible. The wife's consent is not required because the right belongs to the husband based on the verse 'their husbands are more entitled to take them back'. The effect from Ibn 'Abbas and others confirms that the period of raj'ah is the 'iddah after one or two revocable divorces.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The husband's taking back his wife during the 'iddah after a revocable divorce.
طلاق رجعى (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce that does not end the marriage until the 'iddah expires; the husband can take her back without a new marriage.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce during which the woman cannot remarry and the husband can take her back.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only if the wife agrees within the 'iddah.
B) At any time, even after the 'iddah.
C) Only during the 'iddah, regardless of her consent.
D) Only if the divorce was given under duress.

Key Takeaway
The husband's right of raj'ah is a unilateral, revocable entitlement during the iddah after one or two revocable divorces, supported by Quran and Sunnah.

Actions That Constitute Revocation
Enumeration of revocation acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six actions that effect revocation (raj'ah) in the Hanafi madhhab and explain the legal reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Revocation can be effected by explicit words or by intimate actions with desire—actions imply the intent.

In the Hanafi madhhab, a husband's revocation of a revocable divorce is not limited to verbal declaration. This passage from al-Sameri enumerates six distinct actions that constitute raj'ah. Understanding these actions and the underlying principle of istihsan is essential for correctly applying the law of divorce in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of revocatory acts
The passage divides the six actions into two groups: explicit verbal statements and intimate physical actions. Recognize that verbal declarations are clear in meaning, while physical actions require the presence of desire (shahwah) to be legally effective.
Verbal: 'I have revoked you' or 'I have revoked my wife'. Physical: intercourse, kissing, touching with desire, looking with desire.
2
Analyze the explicit verbal acts
The phrases 'راجعتك' and 'راجعت امرأتي' are considered صريح (explicit) for revocation. They directly indicate the husband's intention without ambiguity.
These are clear and do not require additional evidence of intent.
3
Analyze the intimate physical acts
Intercourse, kissing, touching with desire, and looking at the private part with desire are actions that are exclusive to the marital relationship. The Hanafi school infers that such acts imply the husband's intent to continue the marriage, because he would not engage in them without that intent.
The key condition is the presence of desire (shahwah). Without desire, the action does not effect revocation.
4
Understand the legal inference (istihsan)
The ruling that physical actions constitute revocation is based on istihsan (juristic preference), not qiyas (analogy). Qiyas would require explicit verbal expression, but istihsan recognizes the practical reality that such intimate acts indicate the continuation of the marriage.
This is a characteristic Hanafi approach that considers custom (urf) and implied intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۹۷۱) (۲) اور رجعت یہ ہے کہ عورت سے کہے میں نے تجھ سے رجعت کرنی ، میں نے اپنی بیوی سے رجعت کر لی۔ یا اس سے صحبت کر لے یا اس کو بوسہ دیدے یا اس کو شہوت سے چھولے یا اس کے فرج کو شہوت سے دیکھ لے۔

ان چھ صورتوں میں سے کسی ایک کا ارتکاب کرے گا تو رجعت ہو جائے گی۔

رجعتک اور راجعت امرأتی تو رجعت کے معنی میں صریح ہے اس لئے رجعت ہو جائے گی ۔ اور وطی کرنا، بوسہ دینا، شہوت ہے چھونا، شہوت سے فرج دیکھنا یہ بیوی کے ساتھ خاص ہیں اس لئے ان سے بھی رجعت ہو جائے گی۔ کیونکہ رجعت کرنا نہ ہوتا تو خصوصی حرکت کیوں کرتا۔
[1971] (2) And revocation is that he says to her: 'I have revoked you' or 'I have revoked my wife', or he has intercourse with her, or he kisses her, or he touches her with desire, or he looks at her private part with desire. If he commits any of these six actions, revocation will take place. 'I revoke you' and 'I revoke my wife' are explicit in meaning revocation, so revocation occurs. And intercourse, kissing, touching with desire, looking at the private part with desire are specific to the wife, so revocation also occurs through them, because if he did not intend revocation, why would he do such a specific act.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage defines the actions that effect revocation (raj'ah) in the Hanafi madhhab. It lists six actions: (1) saying 'I have revoked you' (راجعتك), (2) saying 'I have revoked my wife' (راجعت امرأتي), (3) sexual intercourse, (4) kissing, (5) touching with desire, and (6) looking at the private part with desire. The first two are explicit verbal statements that clearly indicate revocation. The remaining four are actions that are only done with a wife in marital intimacy, so they imply revocation; if the husband did not intend to revoke, he would not engage in such intimate acts. The underlying legal principle is that an action that is exclusively permissible with a spouse indicates an intention to continue the marriage. This is based on istihsan (juridical preference) over qiyas, which might require explicit words. The passage clarifies that the husband's intention is inferred from the action itself.

Key Terms
رجعت (raj'ah): Revocation; the act of a husband taking back his wife during 'iddah.
شهوة (shahwah): Desire or lust; the presence of sexual desire in the action distinguishes it from casual contact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Saying 'I have revoked you'
B) Having intercourse with the wife
C) Looking at her private part without desire
D) Kissing her with desire

Key Takeaway
Revocation in the Hanafi madhhab is valid through explicit verbal statements or through intimate physical actions performed with desire, based on the principle that such actions objectively imply marital intent.

Witnesses for Revocation: Recommendation Not Obligation
Recommendation but not requirement of witnesses for revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witnesses for revocation and explain why witnesses are recommended but not required.

Core Idea
Revocation without witnesses is valid; witnesses are only recommended.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from the Hanafi school on the role of witnesses in divorce revocation (raj'ah). The Quranic verse 65:2 commands taking witnesses, but the Hanafi school interprets this as a recommendation, not a condition of validity. You will learn the proof from the Quran and the supporting athar, and understand why the validity of revocation is not contingent on witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking two witnesses upon revocation. According to the Hanafi school, this is mustahabb (recommended). If done without witnesses, the revocation is still valid.
The source explicitly says 'مستحب' (mustahabb) and that without witnesses it is 'صحیح' (valid).
2
Locate the Quranic dalil
The ruling is based on Quran 65:2: 'واشهدوا ذوي عدل منكم واقيموا الشهادة لله'. The verse commands taking witnesses when retaining or separating from the wife.
The command 'واشهدوا' is an imperative form.
3
Interpret the command as recommendation
The Hanafi school interprets the imperative as indicating recommendation (nadb) rather than obligation (wujub). This is supported by the principle that the validity of marriage and its continuation does not require witnesses.
Other evidence includes the athar from Abdullah, which confirms the validity of a secret revocation.
4
Confirm with the athar
The athar from Abdullah (Ibn Mas'ud) states that if a man divorces secretly and revokes secretly, it is a valid revocation. This shows that witnesses are not a condition.
The athar is found in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۹۷۲) (۳) اور مستحب ہے کہ رجعت پر دو گواہ بنالے۔ اور اگر گواہ نہ بنایا پھر بھی رجعت صحیح ہے۔

او پر آیت میں ہے۔ واشهدوا ذوی عدل منکم واقيموا الشهادة لله (آیت ۲ سورة الطلاق ۶۵) اس آیت میں ہے کہ رجعت کرنے پر یا چھوڑنے پر گواہ بنالے، جس سے گواہ بنا نا مستحب ہوا۔ اور اگر گواہ نہیں بنایا پھر بھی رجعت صحیح ہے ، اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن عبد الله قال اذا طلق سرا راجع سرا ذلك رجعة فان واقع فلا بأس وان طلق على نيته وراجع فليشهد على رجعته (ب) (مصنف ابن ابی شیبه ۲۵۲ ما قالوا اذ اطلق سر اور اجمع سراج رابع ص ۱۹۷ نمبر ۱۹۲۱۸) اس اثر میں ہے کہ چپکنے سے رجعت کر لی اور
[1972] (3) And it is recommended that he take two witnesses for the revocation. And if he does not take witnesses, the revocation is still valid. In the above verse: 'And bring to witness two just men among you, and establish the testimony for Allah' (Quran 65:2). This verse indicates taking witnesses upon revocation or separation, making it recommended. And if he does not take witnesses, the revocation is still valid. Evidence for this is in the athar: 'From Abdullah, he said: If he divorces secretly and revokes secretly, that is a revocation. Then if he has intercourse, there is no harm. And if he divorces with intention and revokes, let him take witnesses for his revocation.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah...)
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling regarding witnesses for revocation. The Quranic verse 65:2 instructs to take witnesses when either retaining the wife (revocation) or separating. The commentator clarifies that this is a recommendation (istihbab), not an obligation. If the husband revokes without witnesses, the revocation is still valid (sahih). The proof provided is an athar from Abdullah (Ibn Mas'ud) that confirms the validity of a secret revocation, while encouraging witnesses in case of intent disputes. This aligns with the Hanafi position that witnesses are mustahabb (recommended) for evidentiary purposes, not a condition of validity. The verse's command is interpreted as guidance (irshad) rather than obligation (wujub), because the primary purpose of marriage continuation is not conditioned on witnesses.

Key Terms
مستحب (mustahabb): Recommended; an action that is encouraged but not obligatory.
رجعت (raj'ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce by the husband during the waiting period, resuming the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Invalid (batil)
B) Valid but sinful
C) Valid, but witnesses are recommended
D) It depends on the type of divorce

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, witnesses for revocation are mustahabb (recommended), not a condition for validity; revocation without witnesses is valid.

Definition of Raj'ah and Its Validity Without Wife's Consent
Defines raj'ah actions and proof for unilateral validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six actions that constitute valid raj'ah and explain why the wife's consent is not required.

Core Idea
Raj'ah is a unilateral right of the husband during 'iddah — established by explicit words or intimate acts, no consent needed.

After a revocable divorce, a husband may take his wife back without a new contract or her agreement. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Al-Sharh al-Sameri, defining exactly what actions effect raj'ah and why her consent is irrelevant, based on the Quranic command 'So retain them with kindness'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Raj'ah is the act of reinstating a revocably divorced wife during her 'iddah. The ruling is that it is valid and effective upon certain actions.
The passage lists six specific actions.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites Quran 2:231: 'So retain them with kindness' as the primary proof.
Also an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i is mentioned.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'amsikuhunna' is addressed to the husband, indicating his authority to retain the wife unilaterally — the wife's permission is not a condition.
The athar supports that the husband must provide evidence if he claims raj'ah, showing the claim is his right.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Hanafi position is that raj'ah is valid without witnesses or wife's consent. Some other schools may require witnesses, but this passage does not discuss that ikhtilaf in depth.
The focus here is on consent, not witnesses.
5
Evaluate the thamarah
The practical consequence: a husband can effect raj'ah even if the wife objects, and it counts as a valid return. The 'iddah does not need to be restarted.

[1971] (2) This explanation shows that he can perform raj'ah within the waiting period, not after it. And even if the woman is not pleased, he can still perform raj'ah. Commentary
This passage is an expansion_probe on the definition and validity of raj'ah. It begins by stating the chapter heading, then provides the core definition from the matn: raj'ah is established either by explicit verbal declaration ('I have taken you back') or by certain physical acts (intercourse, kissing, touching with desire, looking at private parts with desire). The commentary then explains that raj'ah is valid only within the 'iddah period, and contrary to some possible assumptions, it does not require the wife's consent. The proof is drawn from the Quranic verse 'So retain them with kindness' (2:231), which addresses the husband and implies his unilateral right to retain his wife during the 'iddah without needing her permission. An additional proof is the statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that if a man claims raj'ah before the 'iddah ends, he must provide evidence (bayyinah). This indicates that the claim of raj'ah is a matter for the man, and the woman's role is not condition. The passage then reiterates the definition in Urdu for clarity, explaining that each of the six actions causes raj'ah: explicit words because they are clear in meaning, and physical acts because they are specific to marital intimacy and necessarily imply the intention to resume the marriage.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The act of a husband taking back his wife during her 'iddah period after a revocable divorce, reinstating the marriage without a new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is a unilateral right of the husband, valid through explicit words or intimate acts, and does not require the wife's consent or a new contract.

Witnesses for Raj'ah: Recommended, Not Required
States that witnesses are recommended but not required for a valid raj'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on witnesses for raj'ah and explain the proof from the Quran and athar.

Core Idea
Witnesses for raj'ah are sunnah, not obligatory — the raj'ah remains valid without them.

In this passage, the author clarifies that witnesses are recommended but not required for a valid raj'ah. The Quranic command is interpreted as a recommendation, supported by an athar from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud. This distinction is crucial for understanding the flexibility the Hanafi school provides in matters of reconciliation during the 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing raj'ah (revocation of divorce). The ruling is that having two witnesses is mustahabb (recommended). The passage explicitly states: 'It is recommended that he have two witnesses to the raj'ah.'
2
Locate the Quranic dalil
The author cites Surah al-Talaq (65:2): 'And call to witness two just persons among you and establish the testimony for Allah.' This verse commands calling witnesses for divorce and raj'ah.
The Hanafi school interprets this command as a recommendation, not an obligation, due to other evidences.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'ashhidu' (call to witness) generally indicates obligation in Hanafi usul, but here it is considered zanni al-dalala because the context allows omission. The presence of a companion's athar showing that secret raj'ah is valid overrides the apparent obligation.
The athar from Abdullah ibn Mas'ud states that if a man divorces and revokes secretly, it is a valid raj'ah.
4
Examine the athar support
The passage quotes an athar: 'If he divorces secretly and revokes secretly, that is a raj'ah; then if he has intercourse, there is no problem.' This confirms that witnesses are not a condition of validity.
Abdullah ibn Mas'ud's statement is a qawl sahabi, which is a valid source for determining the ruling's flexibility.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The mufta bihi position is that while it is sunnah to have witnesses, omitting them does not affect the validity of the raj'ah. This provides ease for couples who reconcile privately during the 'iddah.
Some other schools may require witnesses for validity, but the Hanafi school does not.

And it is recommended that he have two witnesses to the raj'ah, and if he does not have witnesses, it is still valid. [1972] (3) And it is recommended that he make two witnesses to the raj'ah, and if he does not make witnesses, then the raj'ah is still valid. The verse above says: 'And call to witness two just persons among you and establish the testimony for Allah' (Quran 65:2). This verse indicates that witnesses should be called for raj'ah or for separation, so making witnesses is recommended. And if he does not make witnesses, the raj'ah is still valid. There is proof of this in an athar: 'Abdullah said: If he divorces secretly and revokes secretly, that is a raj'ah; then if he has intercourse, there is no problem. And if he divorces with intention and revokes, then let him have witnesses to his raj'ah.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). This athar states that if he revokes secretly, it is a valid raj'ah. And the footnote says: So either retain her and make raj'ah with kindness, or let her be until the 'iddah ends; then the woman is more entitled to herself. (a) Ibrahim said: If he claims raj'ah before the expiry of the 'iddah, he is required to provide evidence. (b) 'Abdullah said: If he divorces secretly and revokes secretly, that is a raj'ah. If he has intercourse, there is no harm. And if he divorces with intention and revokes, then he should have witnesses to his raj'ah.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is an expansion_probe on the recommendation of witnesses for raj'ah. It begins with the matn stating that it is recommended (mustahabb) to have two witnesses for raj'ah, but the absence of witnesses does not invalidate it. The commentary then cites the Quranic injunction from Surah al-Talaq (65:2) which advises calling witnesses when divorcing or revoking, interpreting this as a recommendation rather than an obligation. This is followed by an athar from 'Abdullah (likely Ibn Mas'ud) which recounts that if a man divorces and revokes secretly without witnesses, the raj'ah is still valid; however, if he intends to follow the proper sunnah, he should have witnesses. This reinforces that witnesses are a sunnah but not a condition of validity. The footnote (hashiyah) then adds that during the 'iddah, the husband has the choice to retain the wife with kindness or let the 'iddah expire, after which the woman has full right over herself. It also cites Ibrahim's statement about the burden of proof, and again 'Abdullah's statement emphasizing that while secret raj'ah is valid, having witnesses is preferable.

Key Terms
شهادة (shahadah): Testimony of two just witnesses to the act of raj'ah.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that witnesses for raj'ah are recommended but not required; the raj'ah remains valid even without them.

Disputes Regarding Raj'ah After 'Iddah Expiry
Rules if husband claims raj'ah after 'iddah: wife's confirmation or denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on burden of proof when a husband claims raj'ah after the 'iddah and the wife disputes it, and state why the wife is not required to take an oath.

Core Idea
After 'iddah, the wife's confirmation or denial decides raj'ah; her denial is accepted without oath.

After a wife's 'iddah has ended, a husband may claim that he performed raj'ah (revocation) during the 'iddah but never informed her. How is such a dispute resolved? This passage presents two clear scenarios: if the wife confirms the claim, the raj'ah is valid; if she denies, her word is taken—and notably, she does not need to swear an oath according to Abu Hanifa. The lesson explores the rationale and the evidence from the early tabi'un.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage first describes when the wife confirms the husband's claim; then when she denies it. Note the different ruling in each case.
Both scenarios occur after the 'iddah has expired.
2
Extract the ruling for denial
When the wife denies, the husband is the claimant and must bring evidence. If he cannot, the wife's statement is accepted without her taking an oath, according to Abu Hanifa.
This is an exception to the general rule that a defendant may be asked to swear an oath.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i stating that the claimant of raj'ah must provide evidence. This shifts the burden to the husband and supports accepting the wife's denial without oath.
The athar is cited from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
4
Understand the Hanafi principle on oaths
Abu Hanifa holds that in five specific types of claims, the defendant is not required to take an oath; this case is one of them. The commentary mentions that the husband is the claimant and must bring bayyinah.
Other schools may require the wife to swear an oath.

When the 'iddah has expired and the husband says, 'I had revoked during the 'iddah' and she confirms him, then it is a raj'ah. [1974] (5) And if she denies him, then her statement is accepted and there is no oath upon her according to Abu Hanifa. [1973] (4) If the 'iddah has expired and the husband says, 'I had revoked you during the 'iddah' and the wife confirms that, then it is a raj'ah. The wife's 'iddah has expired and the husband says, 'I had revoked you during the 'iddah' and the wife confirms, 'Yes, you had revoked during the 'iddah', then raj'ah will take place. After the wife's confirmation, it becomes established that he indeed revoked, so the raj'ah will be accepted. (5) And if she denies him, then the wife's statement is trustworthy and she is not required to take an oath according to Abu Hanifa. After the 'iddah expires, the husband says, 'I had revoked during the 'iddah', and the wife denies that. And the husband has no evidence; the wife says 'I deny it' and she has evidence? Actually, the wife's statement is accepted. The husband is the claimant and the wife is the defendant, and the claimant must provide evidence; if he has no evidence, then the wife's statement is accepted. But according to Abu Hanifa, an oath is not necessary because there are five issues in which the defendant is not required to take an oath, and this is one of them. (2) There is an athar from Ibrahim: 'If someone claims raj'ah before the expiry of the 'iddah, then he is required to provide evidence' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). This athar states that the husband must provide evidence, and if he does not have evidence, then the wife's statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an expansion_probe covering two related scenarios after the 'iddah has expired. The first scenario (paragraph 4) is when the husband claims that he had performed raj'ah during the 'iddah but did not inform the wife, and the wife now confirms this claim. In this case, the confirmation is taken as proof, and the raj'ah is considered valid, reinstating the marriage. The second scenario (paragraph 5) is when the husband makes the same claim but the wife denies it. The ruling is that the wife's denial is accepted (without her needing to take an oath) according to Abu Hanifa, because the husband is the claimant and must produce evidence. The commentary explains that Abu Hanifa holds that in five specific types of claims, the defendant is not required to take an oath, and this is one of them. The proof for the burden of proof being on the husband is from the athar of Ibrahim, which states that if a man claims raj'ah before the 'iddah ends, he must bring evidence.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or proof, specifically two just witnesses or strong indication, required from the claimant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The husband must take an oath

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband claims raj'ah after 'iddah and the wife denies, the burden of proof lies on the husband, and the wife's denial is accepted without her taking an oath.

Raj'ah Invalidated by Wife's Claim of Expired 'Iddah (Abu Hanifa)
Ruling: Raj'ah invalid if wife claims 'iddah expired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife claims her 'iddah has expired upon the husband's declaration of raj'ah, and explain the condition that invalidates the raj'ah.

Core Idea
The wife's immediate claim that her 'iddah expired overrides the husband's raj'ah.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from Hanafi fiqh: the husband declares raj'ah, and the wife immediately responds that her 'iddah has already ended. According to Abu Hanifa, this response makes the raj'ah invalid. We will analyze the ruling, identify the key condition, and understand why the wife's statement is given precedence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the act is the husband's declaration of raj'ah (revocation of divorce). According to Abu Hanifa, the ruling is that it is batil (invalid) if the wife claims her 'iddah has expired.
Raj'ah is the husband's right to take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new marriage contract.
2
Locate the condition
The condition for valid raj'ah is that the 'iddah period must still be ongoing. The wife's claim challenges this condition.
The wife is the most knowledgeable about her own 'iddah.
3
Note the dispute
This ruling is specific to Abu Hanifa's view; other schools may treat the husband's declaration as sufficient regardless of the wife's claim.
The dispute centers on whose word is given precedence when there is a conflict.
4
Apply the ruling to similar cases
Consider if the wife had not responded or if there is evidence contradicting her claim. The ruling may change based on the strength of evidence.
This passage only covers the immediate response scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قال الزوج قد راجعتك فقالت مجيبة له قد انقضت عدتى [۱۹۷۵) (۶) تو رجعت صحیح نہیں ہوگی امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک۔ شوہر نے کہا میں نے رجعت کر لی، اسی وقت عورت نے جواب دیا کہ میری عدت گزر چکی ہے تو امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک رجعت صحیح نہیں ہوگی۔

Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement presenting a specific scenario: the husband declares raj'ah, and the wife immediately responds that her 'iddah has already expired. According to Abu Hanifa, this response invalidates the raj'ah. The reasoning is based on the principle that the wife's statement is given weight when it contradicts the husband's claim, as she is considered to know better about the expiry of her own 'iddah. The passage does not provide a proof but simply states the ruling. This is consistent with the broader principle in the Hanafi school that the wife's claim about her 'iddah is accepted when there is a conflict, especially if the husband's declaration comes after the putative expiry; the immediacy of the wife's response indicates that the 'iddah had indeed ended, making the raj'ah ineffective.

Key Terms
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.
رجعة (raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his divorced wife during the 'iddah without a new marriage contract.
باطل (batil): Void; a legal act that has no effect from the beginning due to the absence of a condition or presence of a prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The wife's immediate claim that her 'iddah has expired invalidates the husband's raj'ah according to Abu Hanifa, because her word is given precedence over his.

Rajʿah Confirmed After ʿIddah by Wife's Acknowledgment
Ruling on validity of rajʿah after ʿiddah with wife's confirmation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a post-ʿiddah claim of rajʿah when the wife confirms it and explain why it is valid.

Core Idea
Wife's confirmation establishes rajʿah during ʿiddah, making it valid even after ʿiddah expires.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a husband claims he performed rajʿah (revocation) during the ʿiddah period, but he raises the claim after the ʿiddah has already ended. If the wife confirms his claim, the rajʿah is valid. The wife's acknowledgment is sufficient proof; no witnesses are required. This ruling is a straightforward application of the principle that the wife is the most credible witness to her own ʿiddah and events within it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its timing
Determine what act is being claimed (rajʿah) and when it is claimed to have occurred (during ʿiddah). The claim is made after the ʿiddah has expired.
Rajʿah is only effective if done before ʿiddah ends. The wife's confirmation establishes that it indeed happened during ʿiddah.
2
Analyze the role of confirmation
The wife's confirmation (tasdiq) is the key evidence. Hanafi law accepts her word over the husband's bare claim or absence of witnesses.
Contrast with schools that require witnesses; here the wife's knowledge suffices because she is directly aware of the ʿiddah's timeline.
3
State the legal outcome
The ruling: the rajʿah is valid (sahih). The marriage is revived from the moment of the claimed rajʿah.
If the wife denied the husband's claim, the rajʿah would not be established, and the marriage would be dissolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا انقضت العدة فقال الزوج قد كنت راجعتها في العدة فصدقته فهي رجعة
And if the ʿiddah expires, and the husband says, 'I had taken you back during the ʿiddah,' and she confirms him, then it is a valid rajʿah.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This is a clear ruling statement: if a husband claims he performed rajʿah (revocation) during the ʿiddah period after the ʿiddah has ended, and the wife confirms his claim, then the rajʿah is legally valid. The confirmation from the wife establishes that the revocation occurred within the ʿiddah, which is the only time it is effective. The text does not require witnesses here; the wife's acknowledgment suffices. This is based on the principle that the wife is the one who knows best about the passage of her ʿiddah and the occurrence of events within it.

Key Terms
الرجعة (ar-rajʿah): The husband's act of taking back his wife after a revocable divorce before the ʿiddah expires.
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period after divorce or death before a woman can remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) No, because rajʿah must be done before ʿiddah ends.
C) Yes, only if there were witnesses.
D) No, because the husband's claim alone is not enough.

Key Takeaway
The wife's confirmation is sufficient to establish that rajʿah occurred within the ʿiddah, making the marriage revival valid even after the ʿiddah has expired.

Wife's Denial of Rajʿah After ʿIddah: No Oath Required
Ruling when wife denies husband's claim of rajʿah after ʿiddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife denies a husband's claim of rajʿah after ʿiddah and identify the procedural consequence (no oath).

Core Idea
When a wife denies rajʿah after ʿiddah, her word is taken without oath.

In marital disputes over whether a husband revoked a divorce (rajʿah) during the ʿiddah period, a specific procedural rule applies when the wife denies the claim after the ʿiddah has ended. This lesson examines Abu Hanifa's position that the wife's denial is accepted without requiring her to take an oath, based on the principle that the husband is the claimant bearing the burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize the scenario: a husband claims he performed rajʿah during the ʿiddah, but the wife denies this. The claim is made after the ʿiddah has ended.
The timing is crucial: the ruling applies when the ʿiddah has already expired.
2
Determine who is the claimant
In Hanafi legal procedure, the husband is considered the claimant (muddaʿi) because he is asserting a novel fact. The wife is the defendant (munkir) who denies.
Burden of proof generally rests on the claimant.
3
Recall the default rule for defendants
Typically, a defendant's denial is accepted if supported by an oath. However, there are five exceptions where no oath is required. This case is one of them.
The five exceptions are discussed in Hanafi legal maxims.
4
Apply the exception
Per Abu Hanifa, the wife's denial is accepted without an oath. The husband must provide evidence (bayyinah) to support his claim; if he has none, the wife's statement prevails.
This ruling is specific to Abu Hanifa; the two Sahibayn may differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كذبته فالقول قولها ولا يمين عليها عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى
And if she denies him, then the sayings are her sayings, and there is no oath upon her according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This ruling addresses the scenario where the wife denies the husband's claim of rajʿah during the ʿiddah after the ʿiddah has ended. According to Abu Hanifa, the wife's statement is accepted without requiring her to take an oath. The reason is that there are five cases where the defendant (munkir) is not required to take an oath, and this is one of them. The husband is the claimant (muddaʿi) and must produce evidence; if he has none, the wife's denial is accepted. The athar from Ibrahim al-Nakhaʿi supports this: the husband must bring proof (bayyinah) for his claim of rajʿah before the end of ʿiddah.

Key Terms
يمين (yamīn): Oath taken by a party in a legal dispute, here the defendant (wife) to support denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute where a husband claims rajʿah after ʿiddah and the wife denies, what does Abu Hanifa rule?
A) The wife must swear an oath.
B) The husband's claim is accepted.
C) The wife's statement is accepted without oath.
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
When a wife denies a husband's claim of rajʿah after iddah, her denial is accepted without an oath because the husband bears the burden of proof.

Conflict in Rajʿah: Husband Claims, Wife Denies ʿIddah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on simultaneous claim of rajʿah and expiry of ʿiddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two opposing Hanafi positions on the validity of rajʿah when the wife claims her iddah has expired, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A wife's claim that her iddah expired invalidates the husband's rajʿah according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn uphold the marriage.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement about the timing of rajʿah. When the husband says 'I take you back,' and the wife immediately responds that her waiting period (ʿiddah) has already expired, does the marriage revive? Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) give different answers. Understanding their reasoning and the later school's preference is essential for issuing correct fatwas in such cases.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of rajʿah when the wife claims iddah expiry.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi opposition
Note that the Sahibayn are the two companions of Abu Hanifa and they differ from him here.
3
Follow each side's argument
Understand the reasoning: Abu Hanifa accepts the wife's statement as proof that iddah ended before; the Sahibayn take the appearance that iddah was still ongoing at the husband's declaration.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamaratal khilaf)
See how the difference affects the marital status: if invalid, the couple is separated; if valid, the marriage is restored.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify which view is relied upon in later Hanafi fatwa practice, and why.
Later jurists prefer the Sahibayn's view to avoid breaking the marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قال الزوج قد راجعتك فقالت مجيبة له قد انقضت عدتي لم تصح الرجعة عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وقال الصاحبان: تصح
And if the husband says, 'I have taken you back,' and she responds to him, 'My ʿiddah has expired,' the rajʿah is not valid according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: It is valid.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The husband declares rajʿah, and the wife immediately responds that her ʿiddah has already expired. Abu Hanifa rules that the rajʿah is invalid because the wife's statement indicates that the iddah ended before the husband's declaration, so the rajʿah occurred after the iddah, which is ineffective. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the rajʿah is valid because the natural understanding is that the iddah had not yet expired at the time the husband spoke; the wife's response is a subsequent event. The practical consequence (thamaratal khilaf) is whether the couple can continue their marriage. According to Abu Hanifa, if the iddah already expired, they are separated; according to the Sahibayn, the rajʿah revives the marriage.

Key Terms
الصاحبان (as-ṣāḥibān): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi ruling in the Hanafi school, what is the validity of rajʿah when the wife claims her iddah has expired?
A) Rajʿah is invalid.
B) Rajʿah is valid.
C) Rajʿah is suspended until investigation.
D) The case is referred to the imam.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa practice, the Sahibayn's view is preferred, making the rajʿah valid even if the wife claims iddah expiry, to maintain the marriage.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Slave Wife's Raj'ah — Master vs. Slave's Word
Dispute over whose word is accepted in raj'ah of slave wife between master and slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over whose statement is accepted in the raj'ah of a slave wife and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa accepts the slave's denial; the Sahibayn accept the master's confirmation — two competing priorities: personal knowledge vs. ownership of marriage rights.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. A husband claims he performed raj'ah (taking back his wife during her 'iddah) after the 'iddah has expired. The master confirms the husband's claim, but the slave wife denies it. Whose word is taken? Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to weigh competing legal interests: direct personal knowledge versus legal ownership.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
Identify the parties: husband, slave wife, master. The husband's claim is made after the 'iddah has ended. The master confirms, the slave denies. The question: whose statement is accepted to determine whether raj'ah actually occurred?
2
Identify the two positions
Abu Hanifa says: 'The statement is that of the slave woman' (al-qawlu qawluhā). The Sahibayn say: 'The statement is that of the master' (al-qawlu qawlu al-mawlā).
These are clear opposite rulings.
3
Analyze each position's rationale
Abu Hanifa: The slave woman knows best about her own 'iddah — she experiences it personally. The master has no direct knowledge of her 'iddah. The Sahibayn: The master is the owner of the right of marriage (buda') after divorce; he is like a defendant against the husband's claim, so his statement prevails.
Abu Hanifa prioritizes knowledge; Sahibayn prioritize legal standing.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the slave's statement is accepted (Abu Hanifa), her denial invalidates raj'ah — marriage ends. If the master's statement is accepted (Sahibayn), the marriage is restored. This affects future marriages, inheritance, and paternity of children.
The practical consequence is a different marital status.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The source does not give an explicit tarjih, but later Hanafi texts (e.g., al-Hidayah) often prefer the Sahibayn's view because the master has standing to litigate as the owner of the marriage right.
Always check later authorities for the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا قال زوج الأمة بعد انقضاء عدتها قد كنت راجعتك في العدة فصدقه المولى وكذبته الأمة فالقول قولها عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وقال الصاحبان: القول قول المولى
And if the husband of a slave woman says after the expiry of her ʿiddah, 'I had taken you back during the ʿiddah,' and the master confirms him, but the slave woman denies him, then the statement is hers according to Abu Hanifa. And the two companions say: The statement is of the master.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This is another intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a slave wife (umm al-walad or regular slave). The husband claims rajʿah after iddah; the master confirms the claim, but the slave wife denies. Abu Hanifa holds that the slave wife's statement is accepted because she is the one who knows her iddah best; the master has no direct knowledge of her iddah. The Sahibayn hold that the master's statement is accepted because the master is the owner of the slave's right of marriage (buda) and the husband is claiming a right (rajʿah), so the master is the defendant and his denial (or confirmation) is accepted. The practical difference is whether the marriage continues (if rajʿah is valid) or not.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave who is married; here, the slave wife whose marriage is in question.
الرجعة (al-rajʿah): The husband's right to take back his wife during her ʿiddah after a revocable divorce without a new marriage contract.
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period after divorce or death before a woman can remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the intra-Hanafi dispute over raj'ah of a slave wife, what is the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's views?
A) The financial compensation paid to the master differs.
B) The number of witnesses required changes.
C) The marriage is either restored or dissolved.
D) The slave's status as umm walad changes.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a slave wife's own testimony about her iddah is weighed against her master's, leading to different rulings on the validity of raj'ah.

When Does Rajʿah Become Irrevocable?
Ruling on when ʿiddah ends after the third menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conditions under which ʿiddah ends and rajʿah becomes irrevocable, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani regarding the necessity of prayer after tayammum.

Core Idea
If blood stops for 10 days, iddah ends even without ghusl; if less, you need ghusl or tayammum with prayer (or tayammum alone per Muhammad).

This passage from al-Sharh al-Sameri addresses a critical moment in family law: when exactly does a woman's waiting period (ʿiddah) after a revocable divorce end, making reconciliation impossible? The Hanafi school provides a detailed rule, but there is an internal debate among its founding imams about the role of tayammum and prayer. Understanding this nuance is essential for correct fatwa practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text distinguishes between blood stopping for a full 10 days versus less than 10 days. The 10-day mark is the maximum duration of menstruation in the Hanafi school.
The 10-day rule is based on the principle that the most a woman can menstruate is 10 days; beyond that it is istihadah.
2
Apply the agreed ruling for 10 days
If blood stops for 10 consecutive days from the third menstruation, the iddah is complete immediately, regardless of whether she has performed ghusl. Rajʿah becomes impossible.
This is the unanimous position within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the dispute for less than 10 days
When blood stops for fewer than 10 days, the iddah does not end automatically. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) require either ghusl (if water is available) or tayammum followed by prayer. Imam Muhammad holds that tayammum alone suffices, even without prayer.
The disagreement centers on whether the substitution of tayammum for ghusl is complete without the accompanying prayer.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah)
The difference affects the exact moment when iddah ends. According to Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf, the woman remains in iddah until she prays after tayammum; according to Muhammad, she is free as soon as she performs tayammum.
In practice, the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) often follows the majority view, but the minority view is considered weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا انقطع الدم من الحيضة الثالثة لعشرة أيام انقطعت الرجعة وانقضت عدتها وإن لم تغتسل. وإن انقطع الدم لأقل من عشرة أيام لم تنقطع الرجعة حتى تغتسل أو يمر عليها وقت صلاة أو تتيمم وتصلي عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف. وقال محمد: إذا تيممت انقطعت الرجعة وإن لم تصل
And if the blood stops from the third menstruation for ten days, the rajʿah is cut off and her ʿiddah is completed, even if she does not perform ghusl. And if the blood stops for less than ten days, the rajʿah is not cut off until she performs ghusl, or a prayer time passes over her, or she performs tayammum and prays according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: If she performs tayammum, the rajʿah is cut off even if she does not pray.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This ruling defines when the ʿiddah of a woman divorced by a revocable divorce definitively ends, thus making rajʿah impossible. The maximum period of menstruation is 10 days (according to Hanafi). If the blood stops for a full 10 days after the third menstruation, the iddah is complete even if she hasn't bathed (ghusl). If the blood stops for less than 10 days, the iddah is not yet complete; the woman is still considered to be in iddah until she either performs ghusl (to become pure from menstruation), or a complete prayer time passes without her being able to pray (due to lack of ghusl), or she performs tayammum and prays. There is a dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf require both tayammum and prayer for the iddah to end via tayammum; Imam Muhammad holds that tayammum alone suffices, even without prayer.

Key Terms
الحيضة الثالثة (al-ḥayḍah ath-thālithah): The third menstrual cycle after a revocable divorce.
التيمم (at-tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wuḍūʾ or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, what must a woman do to end her iddah if her menstrual blood stops for less than 10 days?
A) Only perform ghusl.
B) Wait until a full 10 days have passed from the start of bleeding.
C) Perform tayammum and then pray, or perform ghusl.
D) Simply perform tayammum.

Key Takeaway
The end of iddah after the third menstruation depends on whether blood stops for a full 10 days; if less, the woman must purify and pray (or pass a prayer time) per the majority of Hanafi imams.

Completion of Iddah and the Ten-Day Threshold
Rulings on iddah completion with intra-Hanafi details

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for when a divorced woman's iddah ends upon cessation of menses, applying the ten-day criterion and the hadith proof.

Core Idea
If her third menses stops at ten days, iddah ends immediately — no ghusl needed.

This lesson examines a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh: the moment a woman's iddah completes after a revocable divorce. The text clarifies when the waiting period ends, drawing on a hadith that defines the maximum duration of menstruation. Understanding this rule is crucial for determining the validity of raj'ah (revocation) and the woman's freedom to remarry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the completion of iddah after the third menstruation. The ruling is that if blood stops at exactly ten days, the iddah ends at that moment, even without ghusl.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith from Abu Umamah establishes that the maximum menstruation is ten days; thus cessation at ten days is a complete menses.
The hadith is used to define the ten-day limit.
3
Understand the condition for less than ten days
If blood stops before ten days, the iddah does not end until she performs ghusl, or a prayer time passes, or she performs tayammum and prays according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
4
Note the exception for a missed limb in ghusl
If she performs ghusl but forgets to wash an entire limb or more, the iddah continues. If less than a limb, it ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم تصح الرجعة عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وإذا قال زوج الأمة بعد انقضاء عدتها قد كنت راجعتك في العدة فصدقه المولى وكذبته الأمة فالقول قولها عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى. وإذا انقطع الدم من الحيضة الثالثة لعشرة أيام انقطعت الرجعة وانقضت عدتها وإن لم تغتسل. وإن انقطع الدم لأقل من عشرة أيام لم تنقطع الرجعة حتى تغتسل أو يمضي عليها وقت صلاة أو تتيمم وتصلي عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله. وقال محمد رحمه الله تعالى: إذا تيممت المرأة انقطعت الرجعة وإن لم تصل. وإن اغتسلت ونسيت شيئاً من بدنها لم يصبه الماء، فإن كان عضواً كاملاً فما فوقه لم تنقطع الرجعة، وإن كان أقل من عضو انقطعت الرجعة. عن أبي أمامة قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: أقل ما يكون من الحيض للجارية والبكر الثيب ثلاث، وأكثر ما يكون من المحيض عشرة أيام، فإذا رأت الدم أكثر من عشرة أيام فهي مستحاضة.
Revocation (raj'ah) is not valid according to Abu Hanifa may Allah have mercy on him. And if the husband of a slave woman says after the expiry of her iddah: 'I had revoked my divorce (raj'ah) during the iddah,' and the master confirms him but the slave woman denies him, then the statement is hers (the slave woman's) according to Abu Hanifa may Allah have mercy on him. And if the blood ceases from the third menstruation for ten days, the raj'ah ends and her iddah is complete even if she has not performed ghusl. And if the blood ceases for less than ten days, the raj'ah does not end until she performs ghusl, or a time for prayer passes over her, or she performs tayammum and prays, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf may Allah have mercy on them. Muhammad may Allah have mercy on him said: If the woman performs tayammum, the raj'ah ends even if she does not pray. And if she performs ghusl but forgets a part of her body that water did not reach, then if it is a complete limb or more, the raj'ah does not end; if it is less than a limb, the raj'ah ends. From Abu Umamah who said: The Messenger of Allah may Allah bless him and grant him peace said: The minimum of menstruation for a girl and a virgin and a woman who has been married is three days, and the maximum of menstruation is ten days. So if she sees blood for more than ten days, she is a woman with istihadah.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings on raj'ah (revocation of divorce) and the completion of iddah. It begins with a statement that raj'ah is not valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa in a context where the husband claims to have made raj'ah after iddah but the slave woman denies it; the woman's statement is credited. Then it addresses the situation where a woman's third menstruation stops at exactly ten days: the iddah ends immediately even without ghusl. If it stops before ten days, the iddah is not complete until ghusl, passing of a prayer time, or tayammum with prayer according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, while Imam Muhammad holds that tayammum alone suffices. The next ruling concerns ghusl: if she forgets to wash an entire limb or more, the iddah continues; if less than a limb is missed, it ends. Finally, a hadith is cited establishing the maximum menstruation as ten days, supporting the previous ruling. The passage shows the Hanafi reliance on hadith and the differences among the early authorities within the school.

Key Terms
الرجعة (ar-raj'ah): The right of a husband to take back his wife during her iddah after a revocable divorce without a new marriage contract.
العدة (al-iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death during which the woman cannot remarry; for a divorced woman, it is three menstrual cycles.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Irregular vaginal bleeding that is not menstrual blood; the woman is called mustahadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only after she performs ghusl
B) Immediately when the blood stops, even without ghusl
C) Only after she prays one prayer time
D) Only after she performs tayammum and prays

Key Takeaway
When a woman's third menses stops at exactly ten days, her iddah ends immediately, regardless of ghusl.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Tayammum and Ghusl in Iddah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tayammum and prayer in iddah completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on whether tayammum alone or tayammum with prayer completes the iddah, and apply the ruling on forgetting a limb in ghusl.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: Tayammum needs prayer to end iddah. Muhammad: Tayammum alone suffices.

This lesson focuses on the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the role of tayammum and ghusl in completing the iddah. The text provides two contrasting opinions from the school's founding imams, along with a specific ruling on forgotten limbs during ghusl. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to identify which view is operative for fatwa and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of dispute
When a woman's third menses stops before ten days, does tayammum alone end the iddah, or must she also pray?
2
State the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf
They require both tayammum and prayer for the iddah to end.
Prayer is considered the completion of the purification act.
3
State the view of Muhammad
He holds that tayammum alone is sufficient; prayer is not necessary.
4
Analyze the ghusl ruling
If a woman performs ghusl but forgets to wash an entire limb, the iddah does not end. If less than a limb, it ends.
This is a separate rule about the completeness of ghusl.

When Does Raj'ah End? Conditions and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on the termination of raj'ah with conditions and proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions that end raj'ah according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and explain the dispute with Imam Muhammad.

Core Idea
Raj'ah ends only by ghusl, passage of prayer time, or tayammum with prayer—tayammum alone is insufficient.

In this lesson, we examine the precise moment when a husband's right to revoke a revocable divorce (raj'ah) expires. The classical Hanafi text presents three specific conditions that bring the waiting period to a close. It also highlights an important intra-Hanafi disagreement between the founder and his two chief disciples on the one hand, and Imam Muhammad on the other, regarding the role of tayammum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is the termination of raj'ah (the husband's right to take back his wife). The ruling is that it ends only upon certain conditions.
These conditions are wajib in the sense that they must occur for raj'ah to end.
2
List the three conditions
Name the three conditions from the text: (1) the woman performs ghusl after her waiting period, (2) a complete prayer time passes without her performing ghusl, or (3) she performs tayammum and prays.
These are the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that tayammum alone (without prayer) ends raj'ah. Understand the disagreement and its basis.
This is an ikhtilaf within the school, not with external madhhabs.
4
Understand the dalil
The proof cited is a hadith setting maximum menstruation at ten days, which defines the waiting period. This hadith is used to determine when the waiting period is complete.
The hadith is from Dar Qutni and is classified as zanni al-thubut.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text presents Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's view first, implying it is the relied-upon position. The majority view requires prayer after tayammum.
Fatwa is given according to this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرجعة
لم تنقطع الرجعة حتى تغتسل او يمضى عليها وقت صلوة او تيمم وتصلى عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف رحمهما الله وقال محمد رحمه الله تعالى اذا تيممت المرأة انقطعت الرجعة وان لم تصل
دس دن حیض ہونے کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابي امامۃ قال قال رسول الله اقل ما يكون من الحيض للجارية والبكر الثيب ثلاث واكثر ما يكون من المحيض عشرة ايام فاذا رأت الدم اكثر من عشرة ايام فهي مستحاضة (ب) (دار قطنی ، کتاب الحیض ج اول ص ۲۲۵ نمبر ۸۳۵) اس سے معلوم ہوا کہ زیادہ سے زیادہ دس دن . ہیں۔ اس لئے اس پر خون ختم ہوا تو ختم ہوتے ہی عدت پوری ہو جائے گی۔
Chapter of Raj'ah. Raj'ah does not end until she bathes or a time of prayer passes over her or she performs tayammum and prays according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them. Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, said: When the woman performs tayammum, raj'ah ends even if she did not pray. The evidence for menstruation being ten days is this hadith: from Abu Umamah who said: The Messenger of Allah said: The least duration of menstruation for a young girl, virgin, or matron is three days, and the most duration of menstruation is ten days. When she sees blood for more than ten days, then she is mustahada. From this it is known that the maximum is ten days. So when the blood ceases for her, at the moment of cessation the waiting period is completed.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the conditions for the end of raj'ah (revocation of divorce). The ruling states that raj'ah does not end until one of three things happens: (1) the woman performs ghusl (full body wash) after the completion of her waiting period, which is ten days from the start of menses; (2) a complete prayer time passes over her without her having performed ghusl; or (3) she performs tayammum and prays. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf. Imam Muhammad differs: he holds that simply performing tayammum ends raj'ah even without praying, because tayammum is a substitute for water and thus itself completes purification. The proof for the ten-day maximum for menstruation is a hadith from Abu Umamah, quoted in the commentary. On this basis, when the blood stops within ten days, the waiting period ends at that moment.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during the waiting period of a revocable divorce without a new marriage contract.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
مستحاضة (mustahada): A woman with irregular, non-menstrual bleeding that does not count towards the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, which of the following scenarios ends the husband's right to raj'ah?
A) The woman's menstrual blood stops.
B) The woman performs tayammum but does not pray.
C) A complete prayer time passes without the woman having performed ghusl.
D) The woman performs ghusl after the prayer time has passed.

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah ends only after specific purificatory acts: ghusl, passage of a prayer time without ghusl, or tayammum with prayer.
