Effect of Forgetting Body Parts in Ghusl on Raj'ah
Ruling on incomplete ghusl and its impact on raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on incomplete ghusl due to forgetting and identify whether a forgotten area constitutes a complete limb or less, determining the effect on raj'ah.

Core Idea
Forgetting a complete limb in ghusl invalidates it; forgetting less than a limb does not — the waiting period and right of revocation depend on this distinction.

In this passage, we examine a specific scenario in Hanafi fiqh: a woman who performs ghusl but forgets to wash some part of her body. The ruling turns on whether the forgotten part is a complete limb or less. This distinction determines whether her ghusl is valid and consequently whether the husband retains the right of raj'ah (revocation) during the waiting period. Understanding this precise rule protects against misunderstandings about the validity of ghusl in the context of 'iddah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is ghusl performed by a woman after menstruation to end her 'iddah. The context is whether the husband may revoke the divorce (raj'ah).
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
The passage gives two cases: (a) forgetting a complete limb or more, (b) forgetting less than a limb.
3
Determine the legal effect for each
For (a), raj'ah does not end; for (b), raj'ah ends.
4
Understand the rationale
Forgetting a whole limb means the ghusl is invalid, so the 'iddah is not considered complete. Forgetting a small area is excused because it dries quickly.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a specific forgotten area, classify it as a limb or not and state the ruling on raj'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان اغتسلت ونسيت شيئا من بدنها لم يصبه الماء فان كان عضوا كاملا فما فوقه لم تنقطع الرجعة وان كان اقل من عضو انقطعت الرجعة۔ دس دن سے کم میں حیض کا خون ختم ہوا اس لئے مکمل غسل کرنے پر عدت ختم ہوگی۔ اس نے غسل تو کیا لیکن مکمل ایک عضو پر پانی بہانا بھول گئی تو گویا کہ فسل کیا ہی نہیں۔ اس لئے شوہر کو بھی رجعت کرنے کا حق ہوگا۔ اور ایک عضو سے کم خشک رہا تو چونکہ بہت کم خشک رہا اور اتنے عضو پر جلدی خشکی آسکتی ہے اس لئے اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا۔ اور یوں سمجھا جائے گا کہ غسل مکمل کر لیا اس لئے اب رجعت کرنے کا حق نہیں
And if she bathed and forgot something of her body that water did not reach, then if it was a complete limb or more, raj'ah does not end; and if it was less than a limb, raj'ah ends. Since her menstrual blood stopped in less than ten days, the waiting period would end upon a complete ghusl. She performed ghusl but forgot to pour water over a complete limb, so it is as if she did not bathe at all. Therefore the husband still has the right to revoke. And if less than a limb remained dry, because that small amount is negligible and dries quickly, it is not considered, and it is deemed that she completed ghusl, so he no longer has the right to revoke.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the effect of an incomplete ghusl due to forgetting. The principle is: if a woman bathes (ghusl) but forgets to wash a complete limb (e.g., an entire arm or leg) or more, then she is not considered to have performed a valid ghusl, so the waiting period (and thus the time for raj'ah) is not over. However, if the forgotten part is less than a whole limb (e.g., a few spots), the ghusl is considered valid and raj'ah ends. The commentary explains that since her menses stopped in less than ten days, a full ghusl is needed to end the waiting period. Forgetting a whole limb means the ghusl is invalid, so the husband can still revoke. Forgetting a small area is excused, and the ghusl counts, so no revocation possible.

Key Terms
عضو (udw): A limb such as a hand, foot, forearm, etc. The ruling distinguishes between a complete limb and less.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a woman performing ghusl forgets to wash her entire left forearm (from elbow to wrist), what is the ruling on raj'ah?
A) Raj'ah ends because the waiting period is over.
B) Raj'ah does not end because the ghusl is invalid.
C) It depends on whether she prayed after ghusl.
D) Raj'ah ends only if she washes that part later.

Key Takeaway
For a ghusl to be valid and for the 'iddah to end, every complete limb must be washed; forgetting a whole limb invalidates the ghusl and preserves the right of raj'ah.

Revocable Divorced Woman Must Adorn Herself
Ruling on the revocable divorcee's conduct during iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a revocably divorced woman's adornment during iddah and explain its rationale.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman should adorn herself to encourage her husband to revoke the divorce.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the conduct of a revocably divorced woman during her waiting period (iddah). While common assumption may be that a divorced woman should avoid beautification, the Hanafi school distinguishes between revocable and irrevocable divorce. The source passage, drawn from a classical fiqh text, clarifies that for a revocable divorce, adornment is recommended to foster reconciliation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Read the passage and determine what act is being discussed. Is it wajib, sunnah, or mubah? The text says 'tatashawwaf wa tatazayyan' (adorns and beautifies). The accompanying commentary clarifies it is recommended (sunnah).
The ruling is sunnah muakkadah, not wajib.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is adornment recommended? Because the marriage is still valid until the iddah ends. The husband has the right to revoke (ruju') and resume marital life. Beautification encourages him to do so.
This is a unique feature of revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The author cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. Identify what specific actions are mentioned (kohl, colored garments, not removing outer clothes). This evidence supports the ruling.
The athar is from a tabi'i, not a hadith, but is accepted in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Distinguish from irrevocable divorce
Contrast this ruling with a woman who has received an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. In that case, she must not adorn herself because the marriage has ended. Understanding the difference is crucial.
This distinction is a common source of confusion.

Source Text

This athar indicates that the revocable divorced woman should adorn herself.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the conduct of a revocably divorced woman during her waiting period. Since the marriage is still legally intact, it is encouraged (sunnah/mustahabb) for her to beautify herself for her husband so that he may be inclined to revoke the divorce and resume marital life. The commentary cites an athar from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, who states that she should apply kohl, wear colored clothes (like those dyed with safflower), and adorn herself, but she should not remove her outer garments (i.e., still observe modesty). This is in contrast to a woman who has been irrevocably divorced, who should not beautify herself.

تتزيَّن (tatazayyan): To beautify oneself with jewelry, makeup, etc.

Entering Upon a Revocably Divorced Wife: Iddah Etiquette
Recommended conduct for husband during wife's iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a husband's entry during the revocable divorce iddah and explain the legal reasoning (sadd al-dhara'i) behind it.

Core Idea
A husband must announce his presence to avoid accidental revocation—precaution over desire.

This lesson explores a fiqh ruling on marital conduct during the waiting period of a revocable divorce. While the wife remains in the husband's home, direct access is not unrestricted. The Hanafi school prescribes a recommended behavior to prevent unintended consequences, rooted in a Companion practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling level
The act is a husband entering upon his revocably divorced wife during her iddah. The ruling is 'mustahabb' (recommended) to seek permission or announce his presence.
This is not wajib; failure to announce does not invalidate anything but may lead to unwanted legal effects.
2
Understand the legal cause (ta'lil)
The reason given is to avoid accidental revocation (raj'ah) via a lustful look. If the husband sees her in a state that arouses desire, that constitutes automatic revocation, which would reestablish the marriage and then cause a longer iddah if he divorces again.
Accidental revocation is a serious matter—it can occur even without intent.
3
Recognize the underlying principle
This ruling is an application of sadd al-dhara'i (blocking the means to evil). It prevents an action (barging in) that could lead to unintended legal consequences.
Sadd al-dhara'i is a key Hanafi principle in muamalat and family law.
4
Examine the evidence
The author cites an athar from Abdullah ibn Umar: when he divorced his wife once (revocably), he used to ask her permission when he wanted to pass by. This shows the practice of a leading Companion and establishes the sunnah nature.
The athar is not a marfu' hadith but carries weight as a Companion practice.
5
Apply the ruling practically
The husband should either verbally ask permission or make a sound (e.g., footsteps, cough) to alert her. This gives her time to cover or prepare herself.
This etiquette preserves modesty and prevents accidental revocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is recommended for her husband not to enter upon her until he seeks her permission or makes her hear the sound of his sandals. If the revocable divorced woman is passing her waiting period in the husband's house, he should not enter the house until he takes her permission or gives her notice by making the sound of his shoes, so that it does not happen that she is uncovered and he suddenly casts a lustful look upon her, which would cause revocation, and then if he divorces again, the waiting period will be longer. Therefore he should enter after giving notice to avoid accidental revocation. Commentary
This passage is a ruling on recommended conduct for a husband whose wife is in the waiting period of a revocable divorce. It is mustahabb (recommended) for him not to enter her room without permission or at least announcing his presence by the sound of his footwear. Revocable Divorce Does Not Prohibit Companionship
Ruling on sexual relations during revocable divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sexual relations during revocable divorce and identify the legal consequence of such an act.

Core Idea
Intercourse with a revocably divorced wife is permissible but automatically revokes the divorce.

This lesson clarifies a common misconception: a woman who has been given a revocable divorce is not entirely prohibited to her husband during the waiting period. Intimacy is lawful, but it carries a significant legal consequence – it constitutes revocation (raj'ah), nullifying the divorce and restoring the marriage without a new contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sexual relations (suhbah) during the iddah of a revocable divorce. The ruling is mubah (permissible).
The permissibility is specific to talaq raj'i, not talaq ba'in.
2
Recognize the condition
The permissibility is contingent on the divorce being revocable and the iddah period still ongoing.
If the iddah expires, the marriage ends and intimacy becomes haram.
3
Understand the automatic consequence
Intercourse does not require an explicit intention for revocation; the act itself constitutes raj'ah by operation of law.
This is a key Hanafi principle: the physical act implies revocation.
4
Apply to a scenario
If a husband has intercourse, the divorce is automatically revoked, and the marriage continues without a new contract.
The husband must avoid intimacy if he does not wish to revoke.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement that revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) does not make sexual relations between the spouses forbidden. During the waiting period, the marriage is still valid, so intimacy is permissible. However, any act of intercourse automatically counts as a revocation (raj'ah), meaning the divorce is nullified and the marriage continues without the need for a new contract. This is important because if a husband intends not to revoke, he must avoid any intimate contact.

Key Terms
صحبت (suhbah): Sexual intercourse; in this context, specifically with a revocably divorced wife.
رجعت (raj'ah): Revocation of divorce during the waiting period, which restores the marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Revocably Divorced Woman Should Adorn Herself
Ruling on adornment for the revocably divorced woman with an athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on adornment for a revocably divorced woman and identify the evidence from the athar of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman is encouraged to adorn herself to facilitate reconciliation with her husband.

This lesson examines a unique Hanafi ruling regarding the revocably divorced woman. While many assume she must mourn or refrain from beautification, the Hanafi school holds that she should adorn herself during her iddah. The reasoning is grounded in the nature of revocable divorce – the marriage bond remains intact and the husband can return to her without a new contract. A report from the eminent tabi'i Ibrahim al-Nakha'i provides the primary evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific action being ruled upon? What is the hukm (recommended, obligatory, etc.)?
The act is adornment (tatašawwaf wa tatazayyan). The hukm is sunnah ghayr muakkadah (recommended).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? Is it from Quran, Sunnah, or other?
The dalil is an athar (report) from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, a mursal hadith.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does this dalil prove the ruling? What specific words or actions indicate the recommendation?
The athar instructs her to apply kohl, wear dyed clothes, and adorn. This indicates permissibility and encouragement.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Is there any scholarly disagreement on this ruling?
Some scholars (e.g., Zahiris) may forbid adornment, but the Hanafi school considers it recommended.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi position
Why is the Hanafi position considered correct?
The marriage bond is still intact; the purpose of iddah is to allow reconciliation, not to impose mourning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a revocably divorced woman (muṭallaqah rajʿiyyah) should adorn and beautify herself during her ʿiddah. The reasoning is that the marriage bond is still intact; the husband has the right to take her back (rajʿah) without a new contract. The commentator supports this with an athar (report) from Ibrāhīm al-Nakhaʿī, who states that the woman should apply kohl, wear dyed clothes, and generally adorn herself for her husband, but she does not remove her clothes (i.e., she does not completely undress as before). This indicates that she is still considered his wife in many respects, and beautification encourages reconciliation. The ruling is understood as mustaḥabb (recommended) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الطلاق الرجعي (al-ṭalāq al-rajʿī): A revocable divorce where the husband retains the right to take back his wife during the ʿiddah without a new contract.
تتشوف (tatašawwaf): To adorn and beautify oneself, especially by wearing attractive clothing and cosmetics.

Etiquette of Entering Upon a Revocably Divorced Wife
Ruling on the husband's entry upon his revocably divorced wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended etiquette for the husband entering upon his revocably divorced wife and apply the Hanafi ruling that announcement avoids unintended rajʿah.

Core Idea
Announce your presence to your divorced wife — surprise can lead to unintended return, complicating divorce.

This passage from the matn addresses the etiquette of entering the home where a revocably divorced wife resides during her ʿiddah. While the husband has a degree of authority, the Hanafi school emphasizes respect and caution. The lesson uses an example from a Companion to illustrate the recommended conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the husband's entry upon his revocably divorced wife during her ʿiddah. The ruling is mustaḥabb (recommended) to seek permission or announce his presence.
The word 'yastaḥibb' indicates recommendation, not obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is an athar (narration) from Abdullah ibn Umar, who would seek permission before entering his divorced wife's room.
Ibn Umar's action is a precedent (qawl sahabi) for recommended conduct.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The link between the dalil and the ruling: the Companion's practice is taken as a demonstration of proper etiquette, not an obligation.
The Hanafis hold that actions of Companions are ḥujja when not contradicted.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is no significant disagreement among schools; all agree that announcing is recommended.
The ruling is muttafaq alayh.
5
Evaluate the legal effect
If the husband enters without announcing, he commits no sin but may accidentally trigger an unintended rajʿah if he sees her and feels desire.
Unintended rajʿah can lengthen the ʿiddah and complicate future divorces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحب لزوجها أن لا يدخل عليها حتى يستأذنها أو يسمعها خفق نعليه. عن عبد الله بن عمر قال طلق ابن عمر امرأته تطليقة فكان يستأذن عليها إذا أراد أن يمر.
It is recommended for her husband not to enter upon her until he seeks her permission or makes her hear the sound of his sandals. From Abdullah ibn Umar: Ibn Umar divorced his wife with one revocable divorce, and he would seek permission from her whenever he wanted to pass by.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage deals with the etiquette of the husband entering the home where his revocably divorced wife is observing ʿiddah. The ruling is mustaḥabb (recommended) that he should not enter unexpectedly. Rather, he should either ask her permission or make a noise (like the sound of his sandals) to announce his presence. The reason is to avoid a sudden glance that might arouse desire and lead to an unintended rajʿah, which could then be followed by another divorce, lengthening the ʿiddah. The proof is an athar from ʿAbdullāh ibn ʿUmar, who himself would seek permission before entering his divorced wife's room. This shows the practice of a Companion and is taken as a recommended conduct.

Key Terms
خفق نعليه (khafaq naʿlayh): The sound of his sandals (or shoes) hitting the ground, used as a form of announcement.
يستأذنها (yastaʾdhinuhā): He seeks her permission before entering.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Seek permission or make the sound of his sandals heard.
D) Only enter at night.

Key Takeaway
The husband should announce his presence to his revocably divorced wife to avoid unintended return; it is recommended, not obligatory.

Revocable Divorce Does Not Forbid Intercourse
Dispute with Shafi'i on permissibility of intercourse before raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intercourse with a revocably divorced wife and explain the evidence and the ikhtilaf with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Revocable divorce does not make intercourse haram; it actually counts as taking the wife back.

This passage addresses a practical question that arises after a husband pronounces a single revocable divorce (talaq raj'i): is he permitted to have intercourse with his wife before he formally revokes the divorce (raj'ah)? The Hanafi school, drawing on the authority of early scholars, holds that intercourse is not only permissible but itself constitutes a valid raj'ah. The Shafi'i school, however, insists on an explicit verbal revocation first. Let us examine the proofs and the reasoning behind each position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn: revocable divorce does not forbid intercourse. Understand that the Hanafi school considers intercourse a valid form of raj'ah.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that intercourse is not permissible until an explicit raj'ah is performed. He cites the athar of 'Ata' and 'Umar.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is the athar from al-Zuhri and Qatada: the divorced woman adorns herself for her husband, implying intimacy is allowed. This is a direct textual evidence.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response to the opponent
The Hanafi response is that their own proof (from al-Zuhri and Qatada) is more specific and aligns with the ongoing marital relationship during the 'iddah period.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In the Hanafi school, if the husband has intercourse, it automatically counts as raj'ah and the wife is taken back. In the Shafi'i school, the intercourse would be sinful and would not effect raj'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطلاق الرجعي لا يحرّم الوطء. عن الزهري وقتادة قالا لتشوف إلى زوجها. وقال الإمام الشافعي لا يحل له الوطء قبل الرجعة. قلت لعطاء ما يحل للرجل من امرأته يطلقها فلا يبيتها؟ قال لا يحل له منها شيء مالم يراجعها. وعن عمر مثله.
And revocable divorce does not forbid intercourse. From al-Zuhri and Qatada: they said she adorns herself for her husband. And Imam al-Shafi'i said: intercourse is not permissible for him before rajʿah. I asked ʿAṭāʾ: What is permissible for a man from his wife whom he has divorced without making it irrevocable? He said: Nothing is permissible for him from her until he takes her back. And similarly from ʿUmar.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute on whether a husband may have intercourse with his revocably divorced wife before effecting rajʿah. The Hanafi position, stated in the matn, is that revocable divorce does not make intercourse haram. In fact, if the husband has intercourse, it automatically constitutes a rajʿah (taking her back). The proof is an athar from al-Zuhri and Qatada that the woman should adorn herself for her husband, which implies that intimacy is permissible. Imam al-Shafiʿi disagrees, holding that the marriage bond is suspended until he explicitly revokes the divorce, so intercourse is not permissible. His proof is an athar from ʿAṭāʾ and ʿUmar that nothing is halal until reconciliation. The Hanafi school responds that the athar they cite (from Zuhri and Qatada) supports their view, and that the ruling of permissibility harmonizes with the principle that the wife remains in the husband's home and the marriage is not fully terminated.

Key Terms
الوطء (al-waṭʾ): Sexual intercourse.
الرجعة (ar-rajʿah): Revocation of a divorce, taking the wife back during the 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is haram until she remarries.
B) It is permissible and counts as taking her back (rajʿah).
C) It is permissible only if she agrees.
D) It is makruh but valid.

Key Takeaway
Revocable divorce does not sever the marital tie; intercourse is permissible and automatically revokes the divorce.

Remarrying a Bā'in Divorcée (Less Than Three Divorces)
Ruling on remarrying an irrevocably divorced wife (less than three divorces)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on remarrying a bā'in divorcée (less than three divorces) and identify the Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
A husband may remarry his bā'in divorcée (less than three) during or after her ʿiddah.

When a husband gives an irrevocable divorce (bā'in) that is not the third divorce, the woman is not permanently forbidden. This lesson explains that the man may contract a new marriage with her even while she is still in her ʿiddah, based on the Quranic verse 'Divorce is twice…'. We will unpack the ruling, the evidence, and the Hanafi reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is remarrying a former wife after a bā'in divorce (less than three). The ruling is permissibility (mubāḥ).
The divorce must be bā'in and less than three; three divorces prohibit remarriage without a muḥallil.
2
Locate the dalīl
The proof is Quran 2:229: 'Divorce is twice, so either retain with honour or release with kindness.' The Hanafi school takes 'retain' to include a new marriage contract.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The word 'retain' (imsāk) can mean keeping the wife, which after a bā'in divorce can only be through a new marriage. Since the total divorces are less than three, the bond is not completely severed.
This interpretation highlights that the ʿiddah serves to confirm paternity, and the child could be from the same husband.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
Some schools argue that remarriage must wait until after ʿiddah, but the Hanafi view permits it during ʿiddah. The practical difference is timing.
Both views are legitimate within their own usūl.
5
Determine the muftā bihī
The Hanafi school relies on the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah: remarriage is permissible during or after ʿiddah, with no need for an intermediate husband.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان طلاقا بائنا دون الثلث فله أن يتزوجها في عدتها وبعد انقضاء عدتها. والدليل قوله تعالى: الطلاق مرتان فإمساك بمعروف أو تسريح بإحسان.
And if it is an irrevocable divorce less than three, then he is allowed to marry her in her ʿiddah and after the expiry of her ʿiddah. The proof is the statement of Allah the Exalted: 'Divorce is twice, so either retain with honour or release with kindness.'
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage deals with the case where a husband gives an irrevocable divorce (bā'in) that is less than three (e.g., one or two bā'in divorces). According to the Hanafi school, such a divorce does not make the woman absolutely forbidden. The husband may contract a new marriage with her either during her ʿiddah or after it ends. The proof is the Quranic verse (2:229): 'Divorce is twice, so either retain with honour or release with kindness.' The Hanafi interpretation is that 'retain' can include a new marriage after an irrevocable divorce, as long as the total number of divorces has not reached three. This is because the purpose of ʿiddah is to ascertain paternity, and since the child could be from the same husband, there is no confusion. The ruling is permissibility (jāʾiz).

Key Terms
البائن دون الثلث (al-bāʾin dūn al-thulth): An irrevocable divorce that is not the third divorce, i.e., one or two divorces that became bā'in (e.g., by expiration of ʿiddah before rajʿah or by khulʿ).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only after she has remarried another man.
B) Only after her ʿiddah has expired.
C) During her ʿiddah or after it.
D) Never, because bā'in divorce is final.

Key Takeaway
A bā'in divorce (less than three) does not permanently sever the marriage bond; the husband may remarry her during or after iddah.

Triple Divorce Forbids Until Intermediate Marriage
Ruling on triple divorce: prohibition without intermediate marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a woman divorced three times becomes lawful to her first husband again, and identify the required elements of the intermediate marriage.

Core Idea
After three divorces, a free woman is haram until a valid second marriage with consummation.

This lesson addresses a critical ruling in family law: the absolute prohibition resulting from a triple divorce. The Qur'an and Sunnah establish that a woman divorced three times cannot return to her first husband unless she marries another man in a genuine marriage, consummates it, and is then separated. We will break down the conditions that make this intermediate marriage legally effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its prohibition
Recognize that the act of divorcing three times (or twice for a slave woman) triggers a permanent prohibition (ḥarām) on remarriage without an intermediate marriage.
This is based on Quran 2:230.
2
List the conditions for the intermediate marriage
Note that the second marriage must be valid (ṣaḥīḥ) and must include actual intercourse (waṭʾ). These are the two essential requirements mentioned in the source.
A mere contract without consummation is insufficient.
3
Understand the completion of the prohibition
Once the second husband divorces her or dies, and her ʿiddah expires, she becomes lawful to the first husband with a new marriage contract.
The second marriage must be genuine; a temporary or trick marriage (ḥiyal) is not accepted.
4
Distinguish between free and slave women
A free woman becomes permanently prohibited after three divorces, while a slave woman does so after two divorces. This is a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh.
This difference arises from the difference in the number of divorces that effect irrevocability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان الطلاق ثلثا في الحرة أو اثنتين في الأمة لم تحل له حتى تنكح زوجا غيره نكاحا صحيحا ويطأها ثم يطلقها أو يموت.
And if the divorce is three in a free woman or two in a slave woman, she does not become lawful for him until she marries another husband in a valid marriage and he has intercourse with her, then he divorces her or dies.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage states the well-known ruling regarding the absolute prohibition resulting from three divorces (for a free woman) or two divorces (for a slave woman). After such a divorce, the wife becomes absolutely forbidden (ḥarām) to the husband. She cannot be remarried to him unless and until she marries another man (muḥallil) in a valid marriage, consummates it, and then either that husband divorces her or dies. Once her ʿiddah from the second husband expires, she becomes permissible to the first husband with a new marriage contract. This is based on the Quranic verse (2:230): 'Then if he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband.' The Hanafi school emphasizes that the second marriage must be genuine and not a mere trick (ḥiyal) intended to make her lawful; it must be a real marriage with the intention of permanence, even if it ends quickly.

Key Terms
الحرة (al-ḥurrah): A free woman (as opposed to a slave woman).
ينكح زوجا غيره (yankihu zawjan ghayrah): She marries another man (different from her first husband).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) She must marry another man, consummate, and be divorced or widowed.
C) She must pronounce forgiveness.
D) She must wait a second ʿiddah.

Key Takeaway
A triple divorce renders a free woman absolutely prohibited until she undergoes a valid intermediate marriage with consummation and subsequent dissolution.

The Condition of Tahleel: Valid Marriage and Consummation
Presents the core condition for a woman to be lawful for her first husband after triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two essential conditions for tahleel and explain the Quranic and hadith proofs that establish them.

Core Idea
After triple divorce, she is halal only after marrying another, consummating, and separating.

This lesson unpacks the core ruling of tahleel—the process by which a woman divorced three times becomes lawful for her first husband. We examine the Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 2:230) and the prophetic hadith that explicitly require both a valid marriage and actual consummation. The lesson also covers the application of this ruling to slave women, who become mughallazah after two divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the Arabic/Urdu text to extract the precise conditions that make a woman lawful for her first husband after triple divorce. Note the sequence: (1) marries another man, (2) in a valid marriage, (3) consummates, (4) then divorces or is widowed.
The text uses 'يدخل بها' (enters her) which Hanafis interpret as actual intercourse.
2
Examine the Quranic proof
Analyze verse 2:230: 'If he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband.' Identify how the imperative 'تنكح' indicates a condition and the negation 'لا تحل' shows prohibition until fulfillment.
The Hanafi usul principle is that al-amr yufidu al-wujub (a command indicates obligation) when not overridden by evidence.
3
Examine the hadith proof
Study the hadith from Aisha where the Prophet (ﷺ) required 'tasting sweetness' (yadhuq 'asilataha). This explicitly mandates consummation, not just a contract.
The hadith is narrated in Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud, and Tirmidhi.
4
Note the Hanafi stance on consummation
Understand that Hanafis insist on actual intercourse (not mere seclusion) as the condition. This is based on the hadith's wording and the Quranic implication.
Shafi'is differ: some hold that valid marriage and seclusion (khalwa) suffice, but Hanafis demand actual consummation.
5
Apply the ruling to slave women
The passage also includes a hadith that the divorce of a slave woman is two divorces. Therefore, a slave woman becomes mughallazah (irrevocably divorced) after two divorces and requires tahleel just like a free woman after three.
This is derived from the hadith: 'طلاق الأمة تطليقتان' (the divorce of a slave woman is two).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الرجعة
تحل له حتى تنكح زوجا غيره نكاحا صحيحا ويدخل بها ثم يطلقها او يموت عنها
گزار کر پہلے شوہر سے شادی کر سکتی ہے۔ اور پہلے شوہر کے لئے حلال ہو سکتی ہے۔
دوسرے شوہر سے شادی کرنے کی دلیل یہ آیت ہے۔ فان طلاقها فلا تحل له من بعد حتی تنکح زوجا غیره ) آیت ۲۳۰ سورة البقرة ٢) اس آیت میں ہے کہ تیسری طلاق کے بعد جب تک دوسرے شوہر سے شادی نہ کرے پہلے کے لئے حلال نہیں ہوگی ۔ اور دوسرے شوہر کی ولی کئے بغیر حلال نہیں ہوگی اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عائشة ان رجلا طلق امرأة ثلاثا فتزوجت فطلق فسئل النبي الله اتحل للاول؟ قال لا حتى يذوق عسيلتها كما ذاق الاول (الف) ( بخاری شریف ، باب من جوز الطلاق الثلاث ص ۷۹۱ نمبر ۵۲۶۱ر مسلم شریف ، باب لاتحمل المطلقة ثلاه المطلقها حتى تنكح زوجاغيره ويطاً حالم بفارقتها وتنقصی عد تقاص ۴۷۷ نمبر ۱۴۳۳ ابوداؤد شریف، نمبر ۲۳۰۹ / ترندی شریف، نمبر ۱۱۱۸) اس حدیث مشہور سے معلوم ہوا کہ محبت کئے بغیر پہلے شوہر کے لئے حلال نہیں ہوگی۔ اور باندی دو طلاقوں سے مغلظہ ہو گی اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عائشة عن النبي الله قال طلاق الأمة تطليقتان وقرونها حیضتان (ب) ( ابو داؤد شریف، باب فی سه طلاق العبد ص ۳۰۴ نمبر ۲۱۸۹ ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء ان طلاق الامته تطلیقتان ص ۲۲۳ نمبر ۱۱۸۲ار ابن ماجه شریف نمبر ۲۰۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ باندی دو طلاقوں سے مغلظہ ہو جائے گی۔
Chapter of Return. She is lawful for him only if she marries another husband in a valid marriage, and he consummates with her, then he divorces her or dies from her. Explanation: She can marry the first husband and become lawful for him. The proof for marrying another husband is this verse: 'If he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband' (2:230). The verse states that after the third divorce, she will not be lawful for the first until she marries another husband. And she will not be lawful without the second husband consummating. The proof is this hadith from Aisha: A man divorced his wife three times, she married another, then he divorced, and the Prophet was asked: Is she lawful for the first? He said: No, until he tastes her sweetness as the first tasted. From this famous hadith, it is learned that without consummation she is not lawful for the first husband. And a slave woman becomes mughallazah (irrevocable) with two divorces. The proof is this hadith from Aisha from the Prophet: The divorce of a slave woman is two divorces, and her waiting period is two menses. From this hadith, it is learned that a slave woman becomes mughallazah with two divorces.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on Return (رجعة), but immediately addresses the pre-condition for remarriage after triple divorce. The Arabic text states the core ruling: a woman divorced three times becomes lawful for her first husband only after she marries another man, consummates that marriage, and then is either divorced or widowed. The author then provides the Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 2:230) as primary evidence, emphasizing that the phrase 'until she marries another husband' requires a valid marriage and consummation. Next, the hadith of Aisha is cited to explicitly require tasting sweetness, i.e., sexual intercourse. The hadith from Aisha about the slave woman's divorce is also included to clarify that one of the rulings applies analogously to slave women with two divorces. The commentary explains that consummation is essential and that mere marriage contract is insufficient.

Key Terms
تحليل (tahleel): The process of making a woman lawful for her first husband by marrying a second husband, consummating, and separating.
مغلظة (mughallazah): Irrevocable dissolution of marriage (as in triple divorce or two divorces for a slave woman) that requires halalah from another husband.
عسيلة (asilah): The 'sweetness' mentioned in the hadith, interpreted as a metaphor for sexual intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for a woman to be lawful for her first husband after triple divorce?
A) A valid marriage contract with a second husband only.
B) A valid marriage and consummation with a second husband.
C) A valid marriage with a second husband and a divorce immediately after the contract.
D) A valid marriage with a second husband and the death of the second husband.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires both a valid marriage and actual consummation for tahleel; mere contract is insufficient.

Ruling of a Boy Nearing Puberty in Tahleel
Extends tahleel condition to a boy near puberty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of tahleel performed by a boy nearing puberty and explain the ikhtilaf with the Maliki school.

Core Idea
A boy near puberty can effect tahleel because the condition is intercourse, not ejaculation.

This lesson extends the general rule of tahleel to a specific case: a boy who is close to puberty but not yet an adult. The Hanafi school holds that such a boy's intercourse suffices, based on the principle that the required pleasure does not depend on ejaculation. We will examine the proof from the athar of Ata and the disagreement of Imam Malik.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is tahleel performed by a boy nearing puberty (murahiq). The Hanafi ruling is that it is effective (sahih): the woman becomes lawful for her first husband.
The murahiq is defined as a boy around 12-15 lunar years, capable of intercourse.
2
Determine the essential condition for tahleel
The condition for tahleel is intercourse (wat') that brings pleasure, not necessarily ejaculation (inzal). The text explicitly states that ejaculation is not a condition.
This distinction is crucial: the Shafi'i school also requires ejaculation, but the Hanafi school does not.
3
Locate the Hanafi proof
The author cites an athar from Ata ibn Abi Rabah: when asked about a woman marrying a boy who cannot ejaculate, Ata responded that it makes her lawful. This is a qawl tabi'i (statement of a Successor) used as a legal precedent.
The athar is from Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq; it is considered zanni in thubut and dalala.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Ata's explicit approval indicates that the key is the act of intercourse itself, regardless of the boy's ability to ejaculate. The pleasure experienced by both spouses is comparable to that with an adult.
The Hanafi principle: al-ibaha ma'a al-shart takfi (permissibility with the condition suffices).
5
Identify the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik holds that a boy nearing puberty cannot effect tahleel; only an adult male suffices. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under the Maliki school, a woman must marry an adult to become lawful, while under the Hanafi school, a murahiq suffices.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, as affirmed by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.

(15) And the boy nearing puberty in tahleel is like an adult. Explanation: She can marry the first husband and become lawful for him. The proof for marrying another husband is this verse... [repeated from previous]. (15) A boy near puberty is like an adult in making lawful. Just as when an adult man marries and consummates, the woman becomes lawful for her first husband; similarly, a boy who has not yet reached puberty but is close to it, if he marries and consummates, she becomes lawful for the first husband. A boy near puberty may not emit semen, but both man and woman experience as much pleasure as with an adult man. Emission is not a condition for tahleel; only intercourse is a condition, which this boy can perform. Therefore, his intercourse makes her lawful for the first husband. There is an athar: I said to Ata: 'A woman whose husband divorced her, then she married a boy who did not reach puberty and could not emit—does that make her lawful for her first husband?' He said: 'Yes, in my opinion.' From this athar, it is known that the intercourse of a boy near puberty makes her lawful for the first husband because emission is not a condition; only the pleasure of intercourse matters. Imam Malik says that a boy near puberty's intercourse does not make the woman lawful for the first husband. Footnotes: (a) Aisha narrated... (b) The Prophet said... (c) I asked Ata...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage addresses the specific application of the tahleel ruling to a boy nearing puberty (المراهق). The Hanafi position, as presented, is that such a boy is equivalent to an adult for the purpose of tahleel. The reasoning is twofold: first, the condition for tahleel is intercourse (وطء) that brings pleasure, not necessarily ejaculation. Second, a boy near puberty can perform intercourse that provides the required pleasure. The author supports this with an athar from Ata ibn Abi Rabah, who explicitly states that such a boy's intercourse makes the woman lawful. Notably, Imam Malik disagreed, holding that only an adult's intercourse suffices. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that the essential condition is the act of intercourse itself, regardless of the actor's age, as long as he is capable.

Key Terms
مراهق (murahiq): A boy who has not yet reached puberty but is close to it (around 12-15 lunar years), capable of sexual intercourse.
تحليل (tahleel): The act of making a thrice-divorced woman lawful for her first husband through a marriage and consummation with a second husband.
انزال (inzal): Emission of semen during intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A Quranic verse
B) The hadith of Aisha about tasting sweetness
C) The athar of Ata ibn Abi Rah
D) Qiyas on an adult male

Key Takeaway
For tahleel, the essential condition is intercourse that brings pleasure, not ejaculation; hence, a boy near puberty suffices according to the Hanafi school, contra the Malikis.

Master's Intercourse with Slave Woman Does Not Effect Tahleel
Ruling with proof on tahleel via master's intercourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tahleel via master's intercourse and explain why it does not suffice, citing the Quranic verse and the athar.

Core Idea
Tahleel requires a new marriage contract; intercourse based on ownership does not suffice.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a slave woman who has been divorced by her husband (possibly also her master) cannot become lawful for him through his own intercourse as master. The Hanafi school, relying on the Quranic verse and athar of the Companions, firmly holds that only a new marriage with another man and its consummation can effect tahleel. Understanding this distinction clarifies the boundaries between marriage and ownership in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is a master having intercourse with his slave woman after she has been divorced by her husband. The ruling is that this does not make her lawful (halal) for the husband.
The ruling is not on the act of intercourse itself, but on its legal effect regarding tahleel.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The primary proof is Quran 2:230, which requires 'tankih' (marrying) another husband. Additionally, an athar from Zayd ibn Thabit is cited.
The verse is qati' al-thubut but zanni al-dalala in this interpretation.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does the verse prove the ruling? The word 'tankih' implies a marriage contract, not mere ownership-based intercourse. The master is not a 'zawj' in the sense of a new marriage.
The Hanafi usul principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub, but here the word is a condition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree? Some early jurists (possibly Malikis) might consider the master's intercourse sufficient, but the majority of Companions and later scholars reject this.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, supported by Zayd ibn Thabit's athar and the general principle that milk al-yamin does not equate to nikah.
This is established in Zahir al-Riwayah.

اصحاب النبی الله (ج) (سن للبیهقی ، باب الرجل تكون تحت امته فيطلقها ثلاثائم بیشتر بھاج سابع ص ۶۱۶ نمبر ۱۵۲۰۴) چونکہ پہلے شوہر تھا اب حلالہ کے بغیر آقا بن کر وطی کرنا چاہتا ہے تو حلال نہیں ہے۔
(16) And the master's intercourse with his slave woman does not make her lawful. And if he marries her with the condition of tahleel... There is an athar from al-Hasan: 'It does not make her lawful because he is not a husband.' From this athar, it is known that she does not become lawful for the first husband. (2) The intercourse of a boy nearing puberty [note: this seems a repetition from previous lesson] does not result in emission and he is not fully a husband, so his intercourse does not make her lawful Marginal note: near puberty. (16) And the master's intercourse with his slave woman does not make her lawful for the husband. For example, a slave woman married someone, and her husband divorced her twice making it mughallazah. Then her master had intercourse with her; this intercourse does not make her lawful for the husband until she marries another man and consummates. The master's intercourse is based on ownership (milk al-yamin) and slave status, not through marriage, because marriage with one's own slave woman is not permissible. The verse states that she must marry and consummate to become lawful; thus, the master's intercourse does not make her lawful for the first husband. (2) The verse: 'If he divorces her, she is not lawful for him after that until she marries another husband' indicates that she must marry; the word 'tankih' implies marriage. (3) There is an athar from Zayd ibn Thabit: He said about a man who divorced his slave wife three times then bought her: She does not become lawful for him until she marries another husband. I heard Malik say that several Companions said that. Since the first husband now wants to have intercourse as master without tahleel, it is not lawful.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage clarifies a specific scenario: if a slave woman is married to a man (who may be her husband), and after two divorces (making it mughallazah), her master (who may be the same man if he owned her before marriage) has intercourse with her, that does not constitute tahleel. The reasoning is that tahleel requires a valid marriage contract and consummation; the master's intercourse is based on ownership (milk al-yamin), not marriage. Moreover, a man cannot marry his own slave woman, so the relationship is not that of husband and wife. The author supports this with an athar from Zayd ibn Thabit and a statement from Malik. The key principle is that the Qur'anic term 'tankih' (marrying) implies a new marriage contract, not mere intercourse from ownership.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla): Master or owner of a slave, who has the right of intercourse based on ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because the slave woman did not consent.
D) Because the divorce was not three times.

Key Takeaway
A master's intercourse with his slave woman, based on ownership, does not fulfill the Quranic condition of 'marrying another husband' and thus does not effect tahleel.

Marriage with Condition of Tahleel is Makruh but Valid
Ruling on a marriage contracted explicitly for the purpose of tahleel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a marriage contracted for tahleel and explain why the contract is valid despite being makruh.

Core Idea
A marriage contracted for tahleel is sinful but effective—the condition does not invalidate the contract.

This lesson examines the delicate balance between intention and legal form. While the Prophet ﷺ cursed those involved in tahleel, the Hanafi school upholds the validity of the marriage contract itself. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping how the madhhab weighs moral gravity against legal efficacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Marriage with an explicit condition of tahleel. What is the hukm level? Makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
The passage explicitly states the marriage is makruh.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the hadith in which the Prophet curses the muhallil and the one for whom tahleel is done. This is found in Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut).
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
How does the curse prove the ruling? The curse indicates that the act is sinful (haram or makruh), but it does not invalidate the marriage contract itself. The essential elements of the contract (offer, acceptance, witnesses) are present.
Hanafi usul distinguishes between moral sin and legal validity.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school holds that such a marriage is invalid (batil) because the condition is contrary to the purpose of marriage. The Hanafi response is that the condition does not affect the validity of the contract.
This is a legitimate cross-madhhab difference (ikhtilaf).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In the Hanafi school, if the marriage is consummated and the second husband divorces her, she becomes lawful for the first husband. In the Shafi'i school, she does not, because the marriage itself is invalid.
This shows how the ruling affects real-life family situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۹۸۶] (۱۷) اگر عورت سے شادی کی حلالہ کی شرط پر تو نکاح مکروہ ہے۔ پس اگر اس کو طلاق دی وہلی کے بعد تو پہلے کے لئے حلال ہو جائینگی اگر عورت نے حلالہ کی شرط پر دوسرے شوہر سے شادی کی تو ایسا کرنا مکروہ ہے، تاہم کرہی لی اور دوسرے شوہر نے ولی کر لی اور طلاق نے شرط تو ایسا اور دی تو پہلے شوہر کے لئے حلال ہو جائے گی۔
مکروہ ہونے کی وجہ یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن عبد الله بن مسعود قال لعن رسول الله المحل والمحل له (د) ترندی شریف، باب ما جاء فی الحل والمحلل له ص ۲۱۳ نمبر ۱۱۲۰ (۲) ابن ماجہ شریف میں ہے۔ قال عقبہ بن عامر قال رسول الله الا اخبركم بالتيس المستعار ؟ قالوا بلى يا رسول الله قال المحلل . لعن الله المحلل والمحلل له (ه) (ابن ماجہ شریف، باب المحلل و امحلل له
(17) If a woman is married with the condition of tahleel, then the marriage is makruh. Then, if he divorces her after consummation, she becomes lawful for the first husband. If a woman marries a second husband on the condition of tahleel, this is makruh. Nevertheless, if she does it and the second husband consummates and then divorces her, she becomes lawful for the first husband. The reason for it being makruh is this hadith: Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said: The Messenger of Allah cursed the muhallil (the one who makes lawful) and the one for whom it is made lawful. (Tirmidhi) And in Ibn Majah: Uqbah ibn Amir said: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Shall I not inform you of the borrowed ram?' They said: Yes, O Messenger of Allah. He said: 'It is the muhallil. Allah cursed the muhallil and the one for whom it is made lawful.'
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage addresses the moral and legal ruling on marriages contracted specifically for the purpose of tahleel. The Hanafi position is that such a marriage is makruh (disliked) because it involves a forbidden intention, as indicated by the hadith that curses the muhallil and the one for whom tahleel is done. However, if the marriage is actually performed and consummated, the tahleel is effective and the woman becomes lawful for her first husband. This is because the marriage contract itself is valid; the condition does not invalidate it. The author cites the hadith to show the prohibition, but Hanafi jurisprudence distinguishes between the immorality of the intention and the legal validity of the contract.

Key Terms
محلل (muhallil): The person who marries a woman with the express intention of making her lawful for her previous husband after divorce.
مستعار (musta'ar): Borrowed; in the hadith, 'borrowed ram' metaphorically describes the muhallil who is used for a temporary purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a marriage contracted explicitly for the purpose of tahleel?
A) The marriage is invalid (batil) and does not effect tahleel.
B) The marriage is valid but makruh, and if consummated, it effects tahleel.
C) The marriage is valid and commendable (mustahab).
D) The marriage is invalid only if the condition is stated before the contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school separates moral sin from legal efficacy: a marriage with tahleel condition is makruh but valid and effective.

Master's Intercourse with Slave-Girl Does Not Make Her Lawful for Former Husband
Ruling with proof: master's intercourse and boy's intercourse do not suffice for halala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why both a master's intercourse with his slave-girl and a boy's intercourse near puberty do not make a woman lawful for her former husband after three irrevocable divorces, and cite the Quranic and athar proofs.

Core Idea
Halala requires a valid marriage with a capable husband — neither ownership nor immature intercourse fulfills it.

This lesson examines two scenarios where intercourse does not achieve halala: a master with his slave-girl, and a boy near puberty. In both cases, the missing element is a valid, consummated marriage with an adult man. The Quranic condition 'tankih' requires marriage, and the Hanafi school applies this strictly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers two cases: (1) a master has intercourse with his own slave-girl after she received three irrevocable divorces from her husband; (2) a boy near puberty (murahiq) marries a woman and has intercourse but without ejaculation, and he is not fully a guardian.
2
State the ruling for each
In both cases, the woman does not become lawful for her first husband. The master's intercourse fails because it is not based on marriage; the boy's intercourse fails because it is incomplete and he is not a full husband.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof for the master scenario
Quran 2:230 says 'until she marries another husband.' The word 'tankih' means marriage. The master's right to intercourse is from ownership (milk al-yamin), which is not marriage, so the condition is not met.
4
Analyze the athar for the boy scenario
The athar from Hasan al-Basri states that the boy is not considered a husband because he cannot ejaculate and is not a guardian. Thus his intercourse does not count for halala.
5
Understand the common principle
Halala requires a valid, fully consummated marriage (nikah) with a man who is legally capable. Anything less, whether based on ownership or incomplete maturity, does not fulfill the Quranic condition.

[1985] (16) And the master's intercourse with his slave-girl does not make her lawful for the husband. A slave-girl had married someone, the husband gave her two divorces making it irrevocable. Now the master had intercourse with her. Due to this intercourse she will not become lawful for the husband until she marries a man and has intercourse.
The master who has intercourse does so by right of ownership and because she is a slave-girl; he does not have intercourse through marriage, because marriage with the master is not valid. And in the verse: that one marries and then has intercourse, then she becomes lawful, so the woman from the master's intercourse will not become lawful for the first husband. (2) In the verse: 'Then if he divorces her, she shall not be lawful for him thereafter until she marries another husband.' (3) There is an athar from Zayd bin Thabit: he used to say regarding a man who divorces his slave-girl thrice and then buys her: she is not lawful for him until she marries another husband. And I heard Malik say: that was said by more than one of the Companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him). (Sunan al-Bayhaqi...) Since he was the first husband, now he wants to have intercourse as a master without halala, so it is not lawful.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling and its proofs. The ruling is that if a master has intercourse with his slave-girl who had been previously married and divorced irrevocably (three times) by her husband, this intercourse does not make her lawful for the former husband. The author provides three proofs: (1) The athar from Hasan al-Basri: 'He is not a husband' – the boy near puberty is not a full husband, so his intercourse does not have legal effect for halala. (2) The Quranic verse (2:230): The condition is 'tankih' (marriage) with a different husband. The master's intercourse is not based on marriage but on ownership (milk al-yamin). Therefore, it does not fulfill the Quranic condition. (3) The athar from Zayd ibn Thabit: He explicitly states that if a man divorces his slave-girl thrice and then repurchases her, she is not lawful until she marries another husband. This shows that ownership-based intercourse does not substitute for marriage in establishing halala. The commentary clarifies that the master's act is not marriage because marriage with one's own slave-girl is invalid (the master cannot marry his own slave because he already owns her, and marriage is a contract between two parties, whereas a master and his slave cannot contract a valid marriage with each other in Hanafi law). Thus, the intended effect of making the woman lawful for her first husband is not achieved.

Key Terms
حلالہ (halālah): The process of making a woman lawful for her first husband after three irrevocable divorces by marrying another man, consummating the marriage, and then being divorced or widowed.
وطی (waṭʾ): Sexual intercourse; the consummation that completes halala when done in a valid marriage.
مولی (mawlā): Master/owner of a slave-girl; has the right of intercourse by ownership (milk al-yamin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Intercourse with a boy near puberty who does not ejaculate.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Neither ownership-based intercourse nor incomplete intercourse by an immature boy fulfills the Quranic condition of 'tankih' for halala.

The Second Marriage Resets the Divorce Count
Ruling with proof: after second marriage, woman returns with three divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how a second marriage affects previous divorces and apply the evidence (athar and qiyas) to determine the new divorce count.

Core Idea
A new marriage and consummation nullify all previous divorces; the woman returns with a fresh set of three.

When a divorced woman marries another man and consummates, then returns to her first husband, does she bring back her old divorce count or start anew? The Hanafi school, led by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, says she comes with three new divorces. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the proof from the Companions, and the a fortiori reasoning that supports it.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the ruling
Identify the exact scenario: a free woman divorced once or twice, completes iddah, remarries, consummates, then returns to the first husband. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, she comes with three new divorces.
The previous divorces are completely nullified (hadm).
2
Locate and analyse the primary evidence
The text cites an athar from Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar: 'New marriage and new divorce.' Analyse how this statement supports the ruling — it implies a reset of the divorce regime.
The athar is mursal (from a Tabi'i?) but accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Identify the qiyās (a fortiori) argument
The ruling uses qiyas: if three divorces are wiped out by a second marriage, then less than three are wiped out with even greater reason.
This is qiyas jali (clear analogy) with the illah being the new marital legality.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad holds that only the remaining divorces carry over (e.g., if one divorce was given before, after second marriage only two remain). Understand the difference.
The mufta bihi position is Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
5
Apply the ruling to a concrete example
Take a woman divorced once, then through a second marriage and return: the first husband must give three divorces to make it irrevocable, not just two.
This has practical consequences for assessing when tahlil (halala) is needed.

[1987] (18) If a husband divorces a free woman once or twice, and her iddah passes, and she marries another husband, and he consummates with her, then she returns to the first husband, she will come with three divorces. Because the second husband nullifies three just as he nullifies less than three according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
If a husband divorces a free woman once or twice, she completes iddah, marries another, consummates, then he divorces her, she completes iddah, and then remarries the first husband: how many divorces does the first husband need to give to make it irrevocable? Three divorces or the remaining? For example, if he gave one divorce previously, would it become irrevocable with just two divorces (and need halala) or with three (and need halala)? Similarly, if two divorces were given, would one more make it irrevocable (and need halala) or three? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say: after marriage and consummation with the second husband, when she returns to the first, she comes with full three divorces, and the previous one or two become nullified and are not considered. This is called coming with a new basis (mil jadid). Just as with three divorces, after marriage and consummation with the second husband, she comes with a new legality (hull jadid), similarly with less than three she also comes with a new legality. Because the second husband washes away the severity of three, so a fortiori he washes away the severity of less. (2) There is an athar proving this: from Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar: 'New marriage and new divorce.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, ... Kitab al-Athar ...) From this it became known that in the case of one or two divorces.
Footnote (continued from previous page): They said: Yes, O Messenger of Allah. He said: That is the muhallil and the one for whom tahlil is done. (الف) Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar said: After the second husband, there is a new marriage and a new divorce.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling of Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the effect of a second marriage on previous divorces. The issue: When a free woman is divorced once or twice, completes her iddah, marries another man, consummates, and then (after being divorced by the second husband and completing iddah) returns to her first husband, how many divorces does the first husband need to give to make it irrevocable (ba'in)? The answer according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is that she comes with a completely new marriage and three new divorces. The previous one or two divorces are nullified (kal-'adam). This is called 'mil jadid' (new basis) or 'hull jadid' (new legality). The reasoning is by qiyas (analogy): if a woman who was given three divorces remarries and consummates, she returns with a fresh slate; similarly, if less than three divorces were given, the second marriage wipes them out a fortiori. The evidence is the athar from Ibn Abbas and Ibn Umar: 'New marriage and new divorce.' This means that after a second marriage, the previous divorce count is irrelevant and the first husband's future divorces start anew. The passage also notes that Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani may have differed (but the text does not mention him, only Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). The footnote clarifies that the new marriage brings a new divorce regime. This ruling applies specifically to the opinion of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Imam Muhammad held a different view (that the remaining count carries over).

Key Terms
حل جدید (ḥull jadīd): A new legality; after a valid second marriage and consummation, the woman enters a fresh marital bond with her first husband.
ہدم (hadm): The second husband 'demolishes' or nullifies the effect of previous divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), a valid second marriage with consummation erases all previous divorces, granting the woman a fresh slate of three divorces.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does a Second Marriage Erase Less Than Three Divorces?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of second marriage on less-than-three divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether a second marriage erases less than three divorces, explain the opposing view of Imam Muhammad, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Does a second marriage erase <3 divorces? Abu Hanifa: yes; Muhammad: no — the count carries over.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his student Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. At stake is the effect of a second marriage after less than three divorces: does it reset the divorce count to three, or does the first husband retain the remaining divorces? Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to trace how different proofs lead to different rulings even within the same school.

Guided Path
1
State the two Hanafi positions
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf versus Muhammad al-Shaybani on whether a second marriage erases less than three divorces.
Abu Hanifa: erases completely; Muhammad: only erases three.
2
Identify each side's proof
Abu Hanifa relies on the general principle that second marriage erases 'shiddah'; Muhammad cites an athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab.
The athar states the woman returns 'on what remains of her divorces'.
3
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Work out the practical difference: if one divorce was given, according to Abu Hanifa the first husband now has three new divorces; according to Muhammad he has only two remaining.
This affects whether a subsequent divorce causes baynuna kubra.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
Determine which position is mufta bihi (relied upon) and why: Abu Hanifa's view is from Zahir al-Riwayah and supported by the majority.
Muhammad's view is considered weak in practice.

واذا طلق الرجل الحرة تطليقة او تطليقتين وانقضت عدتها وتزوجت بزوج آخر فدخل بها ثم عادت الى الاول عادت بثلاث تطليقات ويهدم الزوج الثاني مادون الثلث كما يهدم الثلث عند ابي حنيفة وابي يوسف رحمهما الله ۱۹۸۸ (۱۹) وقال محمد رحمه الله تعالى لا يهدم الزوج الثاني لا يهدم الزوج الثانى مادون الثلاث امام محمد نے فرمایا زوج ثانی نہیں کا لعدم کرتا ہے تین سے کم کو ۔ یعنی پہلے شوہر نے تین طلاقوں سے کم دی تو زوج ثانی سے نکاح اور وطی کرنا اس کو کالعدم نہیں کرے گا بلکہ بحال رہے گی اور مابقیہ طلاق دینے کا اختیار ہوگا۔ اثر میں ہے۔ قال عمر بن الخطاب ايما امرأة طلقها زوجها تطليقة او تطليقتين ثم تركها حتى تنكح زوجا غيره فيموت عنها او يطلقها ثم ينكحها زوجها الاول فانها عنده على ما بقى من طلاقها (الف) (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب النکاح جدید والطلاق جدید ج سادس ص ۳۵۱ نمبر ۱۱۱۴۹ار مصنف ابن ابی هیبته ۹۸ م قالوا فی الرجل يطلق امرأتة تطليقتين او تطليقه فتزوج ثم ترجع الیہ علی کم تکون عنده؟ ج رابع ص ۷۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ زوج اول مابقی طلاق کا مالک ہوگا۔
And if a man divorces a free woman one or two divorces, and her iddah expires, and she marries another husband, and he consummates with her, then she returns to the first husband, she returns with three divorces, and the second husband erases less than three just as he erases three according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them). 1988 (19). And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The second husband does not erase less than three. Imam Muhammad said: The second husband does not nullify less than three. That is, if the first husband gave less than three divorces, then marriage and consummation with the second husband will not nullify it; rather it remains and he will have the right to give the remaining divorce. There is an athar: Umar ibn al-Khattab said: Any woman whom her husband divorces one or two divorces, then leaves her until she marries another husband, and he dies or divorces her, then her first husband marries her, she is with him on what remains of her divorces. (From Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-Nikah al-Jadid wa al-Talaq al-Jadid, vol. 6, p. 351, no. 11149; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol. 4, p. 78). From this athar it is understood that the first husband owns the remaining divorces.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other, regarding whether a second marriage and consummation erases less than three divorces. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that it does, so the woman returns to the first husband with three divorces (a fresh start). Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that it does not; the first husband retains the remaining divorces (e.g., if he gave one divorce, he now has the right to give two more). Muhammad supports his view with an athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab, which states that the woman is with her first husband on what remains of her divorces. The Urdu commentary explains that accordingly, the first husband owns the remaining divorces.

Key Terms
هدم (hadm): To nullify or erase the effect of prior divorces by a subsequent valid marriage and consummation.
مادون الثلث (ma duna al-thuluth): Less than three (divorces); i.e., one or two divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, what happens when a woman divorced with one or two divorces remarries, consummates, and returns to her first husband?
A) She returns with three new divorces.
B) She returns with the remaining divorces (e.g., one plus two more).
C) She is permanently prohibited.
D) The first husband must give a new mahr.

Key Takeaway
A second marriage after less than three divorces erases the divorce count only according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Imam Muhammad holds that the count carries over, but the mufta bihi is the former.

Accepting the Wife's Claim of Remarriage After Triple Divorce
Ruling on when a husband may accept his wife's claim of remarriage after triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a Hanafi husband may accept his wife's claim of remarriage after triple divorce and apply the principle of ghalib al-zann.

Core Idea
Husband may believe wife's claim of halala if sufficient time has passed, based on strong presumption (ghalib al-zann).

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a common scenario: a woman divorced three times returns after a period claiming she has remarried, consummated, and been divorced again. The school allows the husband to accept her word if the timeline and circumstances create a strong presumption of truthfulness. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the concept of ghalib al-zann and its role in family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? The husband accepting the wife's claim that she performed halala after triple divorce. The ruling is mubah (permissible) under conditions.
2
State the condition
Sufficient time must have elapsed for the wife to complete the iddah of the first divorce, marry a second husband, consummate, be divorced, and complete a second iddah.
Minimum time frames are provided in the text.
3
Identify the evidentiary standard
The husband must have strong presumption (ghalib al-zann) that she is truthful, based on circumstantial evidence like time and normal possibilities.
Certainty is not required.
4
Apply the condition to examples
For a consummated marriage: 93 days minimum (three menstruations + intervals). For a non-consummated marriage: 39 days minimum.
These are derived from minimum durations of menstruation and purity intervals.
5
Note the practical rule
The husband is not obligated to accept her claim; he may do so if satisfied. The ruling provides flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he divorces her three times, and she says: 'My iddah expired, and I married another husband, and the second husband entered with me (consummated), and he divorced me, and my iddah expired.' Also in this case, the woman comes to the first husband with a new solution and a new marriage. (19) Imam Muhammad said: The second husband does not nullify less than three. That is, if the first husband gave less than three divorces, then marriage and consummation with the second husband will not nullify it; rather it remains and he will have the right to give the remaining divorce. (1989) (20) If he divorced her three times, then she said: 'My iddah passed, and I married another husband, and the second husband had intercourse with me, and he divorced me, and my iddah passed.' And there is probability in the time, then it is permissible for the first husband to believe her if there is a strong presumption that she is truthful. The husband gave his wife three divorces. After a period, the wife returned and said: 'I completed your iddah and married another husband. He consummated with me and quickly divorced me. After completing his iddah, I have become lawful for you; marry me.' So if between the husband's divorce and her return there is enough time for such to happen, and situations indicate that she may have done so, then the first husband has leeway to believe her and marry her. For example, if he gave three divorces to a woman with whom he had consummated, and she returned after 93 days; if he gave three divorces to a non-consummated woman, and she returned after 39 days, then it can be assumed she is truthful. The minimum duration of menstruation is three days, so there are three menstrual periods. And the minimum interval between two menstruations is fifteen days, so between three menstruations there are two periods of purity, so two purity periods make thirty days. Now thirty and thirty make sixty, then there will be a period of purity in which the woman (will marry another).
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the situation where a woman who has been given three divorces claims that she remarried, consummated, and was divorced again, and her second iddah has passed. According to the Hanafi school, the first husband may believe her and remarry her if sufficient time has elapsed for these events to have occurred. The text gives examples: for a consummated woman, 93 days (three menstrual cycles plus intervals) would be minimum; for a non-consummated woman, 39 days (three menstrual cycles for iddah of divorce, plus time for marriage and divorce? Actually, the text's calculation seems to be three menstrual cycles at minimum of 3 days each = 9 days, plus two intervals of purity of at least 15 days each = 30 days, total 39 days for the three divorces iddah. Then for the second marriage, another iddah would be required, but the text seems to indicate 93 days for a consummated woman maybe including the second marriage and iddah? The exact calculation may be further detailed. The husband is not obligated to accept her claim, but it is permissible if he is satisfied. This ruling is specific to the case of triple divorce where the woman claims a complete intermediate marriage.

Key Terms
غالب گمان (ghalib al-zann): The husband's strong presumption or belief in the truthfulness of the woman's claim based on circumstantial evidence (time elapsed, normal possibility).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) She swears an oath.

Key Takeaway
The husband may accept his wife's claim of halala after triple divorce only when sufficient time has passed to create a strong presumption of truthfulness.

Effect of Second Husband's Marriage When Divorces Are Less Than Three
Ruling on the effect of second husband's marriage when divorces are less than three

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that a second husband's marriage and consummation do not nullify the remaining divorces when the first husband gave less than three divorces.

Core Idea
Marrying a second husband after less than three divorces does not cancel the remaining divorces; the first husband retains the right to issue them.

This lesson addresses a specific point from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: if a husband divorces his wife once or twice, and she then marries another man, her subsequent marriage and consummation do not nullify the remaining divorces. She remains married to the first husband in the sense that his power to divorce further is preserved. This clarifies the distinction between the effects of three vs. fewer divorces.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The first husband has given less than three divorces (one or two). The wife then marries another man and consummates.
2
State the ruling
The second marriage does not nullify the remaining divorces; the first husband still has the right to issue them.
This is the view of Imam Muhammad.
3
Contrast with triple divorce
In triple divorce, a valid second marriage and consummation are required for the wife to become lawful again (halala). For less than three, the second marriage has no such effect.
4
Note the practical consequence
If the wife returns to the first husband after the second marriage, she is still bound by the original divorces; the husband may divorce her the remaining number of times.
