intermediate
8 min
Verification of Woman's Claim of Remarriage After Triple Talaq
Explaining ruling on woman claiming second marriage after triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a husband may accept his divorced wife's claim of remarriage and apply the Hanafi ruling based on ghalib al-zann.

Core Idea
A woman's claim of remarriage after triple talaq is accepted only if the time allows and the husband's dominant assumption confirms her truthfulness.

When a woman claims to have completed her iddah and remarried after triple talaq, the husband must assess the plausibility of her timeline and his own conviction. The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of the Companions, provides a detailed framework: minimum iddah durations and the principle of ghalib al-zann. This lesson unpacks the calculations and the proof from the athar of Umar and Ali.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Recognize that the passage deals with a woman who claims to have completed iddah after triple talaq, married another man, consummated, been divorced, and completed a second iddah, now claiming to be halal for the first husband. The ruling states it is permissible for the husband to accept her claim if conditions are met.
Distinguish this from cases of less than three divorces where the second husband does not nullify the remaining divorces.
2
Understand the calculation of minimum iddah periods
For a consummated marriage (madkhul biha), the minimum iddah after triple talaq is 39 days (three menstrual cycles: 3 days each + 15-day intervals between). After remarriage and consummation, the second iddah is 54 days (three more cycles). Total minimum: 93 days. For a non-consummated wife, only one iddah of 39 days is needed before she can claim remarriage.
The passage provides a detailed diagram; internalize that these are minimums based on the shortest possible menstrual and purity periods.
3
Analyze the condition of plausible time
The husband must check whether the time elapsed since the first talaq is sufficient for the claimed events. If the woman returns before the minimum period, her claim is not plausible and cannot be accepted.
Common error: forgetting to add both iddahs; the total for madkhul biha is 93 days, not 39.
4
Apply the principle of ghalib al-zann
Even if the time is plausible, the husband must have a dominant assumption (ghalib al-zann) that she is truthful. This is based on circumstantial evidence, her character, and lack of contradiction.
This is lower than certainty but still a valid basis for action in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Recognize the evidence from the Companions
The passage cites athar from Umar ibn al-Khattab regarding less than three divorces, and from Ali ibn Abi Talib where Shurayh required witnesses for three menstruations. The Hanafi school interprets these to allow acceptance when witnesses are unavailable but time and dominant assumption support the claim.
The athar provide authority for the ruling; note that Ali's statement shows that witnesses are a strong form of evidence, but the Hanafi position adds flexibility.

Chapter of Revocation. And Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on them both) 1988 (19) And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The second husband does not nullify less than three. 1989 (20) And if he divorced her three times and she said: My iddah has ended and I married another husband and the second husband entered me and divorced me and my iddah ended, and the time allows it, then it is permissible for the first husband to believe her if his dominant assumption is that she is truthful. Evidence: Umar ibn al-Khattab said: Any woman whose husband divorced her once or twice, then left her until she married another man and he died or divorced her, then her first husband married her, she is with him on the remaining divorces. Another evidence: From al-Sha'bi: A man came to Ali ibn Abi Talib and said: I divorced my wife and she came after two months saying my iddah has ended. Ali had Shurayh with him. Shurayh said: If she brings trustworthy witnesses from her family that she menstruated three times, she is truthful; otherwise she is lying. Ali said: Qalun (Greek for correct). So it is understood that if the time allows and dominant assumption indicates truth, her statement is accepted.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario: a woman who has been given triple divorce claims to have completed her iddah by marrying another man, consummating the marriage, being divorced, and completing a second iddah, so she is now halal for the first husband. The passage first states Imam Muhammad's position that if the first husband gave less than three divorces, the second husband does not nullify them; the woman remains with her first husband on the remaining number of divorces. Then it discusses the case of three divorces. The ruling is that if a plausible period has passed (calculated based on minimum iddah durations) and the husband's dominant opinion (ghalib al-zann) is that she is truthful, he may accept her claim and remarry her. The passage provides detailed calculations: for a consummated marriage (madkhul biha), the minimum iddah after triple talaq is 39 days (three menstrual cycles: minimum 3 days each with 15-day intervals between). For the second husband's iddah after consummation, it is 54 days (similarly three cycles). Total 93 days. For a non-consummated (ghayr madkhul biha) woman, only one iddah of 39 days is needed. The passage cites two athar: one from Umar ibn al-Khattab regarding less than three divorces, and one from Ali ibn Abi Talib where Shurayh judged that the woman must bring witnesses to her three menstruations. This shows that the claim is accepted only with supporting evidence or strong circumstantial probability. The commentary emphasizes that this is based on the principle that a woman's statement about her iddah is credible when the time allows and there is no contradiction.

Key Terms
رجعة (raj'ah): The revocation of a revocable divorce by the husband during the iddah period.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying.
غالب الظن (ghalib al-zann): Dominant opinion; a degree of certainty less than absolute but sufficient to base a ruling on.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum total iddah period for a woman who was divorced after consummation (madkhul biha) and then married another man, consummated, divorced again, and completed her second iddah?
A) 39 days
B) 54 days
C) 93 days
D) 90 days

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a husband to accept his wife's claim of remarriage after triple talaq only when the minimum iddah durations have elapsed and his dominant assumption favors her truthfulness, as established by the practice of the Companions.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Accepting a Woman's Claim of Completed 'Iddah
Ruling with supporting athar from Ali on verifying 'iddah end.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for accepting a woman's claim of completed 'iddah according to the Hanafi school and explain how the athar of Ali supports this ruling.

Core Idea
A woman's claim of 'iddah completion is accepted only if the timeframe permits and her truthfulness is the dominant presumption.

In Hanafi fiqh, matters of 'iddah often rely on a woman's testimony due to privacy, but her claim is not automatically accepted. This passage from al-Sharh al-Samari presents the ruling that her word is taken if two conditions hold: the time frame could possibly accommodate her claim, and there is a dominant presumption (ghalib al-zann) of her honesty. The proof is an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib, where Shurayh advises that she must bring witnesses to confirm three menstruations. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and conditions
Read the first part of the passage to find the ruling: the first husband may believe her if the timeframe accommodates and his dominant presumption is that she is truthful. These are the two conditions.
The ruling is about accepting her claim; it is not obligatory.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib reported by al-Sha'bi. This is the evidence (dalil) for the ruling.
The athar is a qawl sahabi, which has zanni thubut and dalala.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Examine how the athar supports the ruling. Shurayh's statement 'if she brings trustworthy witnesses...' shows that without evidence, she is not automatically believed. The commentary explains that if the timeframe is possible and there is dominant presumption, her word is accepted. So the athar is interpreted to require possibility, not absolute witnesses.
The phrase 'if the timeframe accommodates it' is key to the Hanafi interpretation.
4
Apply the conditions to a scenario
Practice with a hypothetical: A non-consummated divorcée returns after 50 days claiming her 'iddah of three pure periods is complete. Minimum possible is 39 days, so timeframe accommodates. If the husband has no reason to doubt, dominant presumption applies, so her claim is accepted.
The passage includes a detailed calculation with two marriages; simplify for this lesson.
5
Note the authority and weight
The dalil is from a Companion, classified as zanni in transmission and meaning. The Hanafi school relies on it as mufta bihi under the condition of possibility and dominant presumption.
This ruling is from fatawa mutakhkhirin; it is not from zahir al-riwayah.

اس لئے ۹۳ دن کے بعد عورت واپس آئے تو اس کی تصدیق کی جاسکتی ہے۔

اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ عن الشعبي قال جاء رجل الى على بن ابى طالب فقال اني طلقت امرأتي فجائت بعد شهرين فقالت قد انقضت عدتى وعند على شريح فقال قل فيها قال وانت شاهد يا امير المؤمنين قال نعم قال ان جائت ببطانة من اهلها من العدول يشهدون انها حاضت ثلاث حيض والا فهى كاذبة فقال على قالون بالرومية اني اصبت (الف) (سن للبیهقی ، باب تصديق المرأة فيما يمكن فيه انقضاء عد تاج سابع جی ۶۸۷ نمبر ۱۵۴۰۵ر مصنف ابن ابی شیبه ۲۸ من قال او تمنت المرأة على فرجهاج رابع ص ۲۰۶، نمبر ۱۹۲۸۹) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ مدت اس کا متحمل کرتی ہو اور غالب گمان ہو کہ وہ سچ بولتی ہے تو بات مان لی جائے گی۔ عورت غیر مدخول بہا کو ایک طلاق دی ہے اس لئے صرف دوسرے شوہر کی عدت گزارتی ہے جو کم سے کم ۳۹ دن ہوں گے۔ اور وہ دو ماہ کے بعد واپس آتی ہے اس لئے بچ پر محمول کیا جاسکتا ہے اگر قرائن سے بچی معلوم ہو ورنہ عمومی طور پر تین حیض کے لئے تین ماہ چاہئے۔

حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت شعمی نے فرمایا ایک آدمی حضرت علی کے پاس آیا اور کہا میں نے بیوی کو تین طلاقیں دیں پھر وہ دو ماہ کے بعد آئی اور کہا میری عدت ختم ہو چکی ہے۔ حضرت علی کے پاس قاضی شریح تھے۔ انہوں نے فرمایا قاضی صاحب آپ کہیں ! حضرت شریح نے فرمایا امیر المؤمنین ! آپ گواہ ہیں ؟ کہا ہاں ! حضرت شریح نے کہا اگر اس کے خاص اہل والے عادل آدمی گواہی دیں کہ اس کو تین حیض ہوئے ہیں تو وہ سچ ہے در نہ تو وہ جھوٹی ہے۔ پس حضرت علی نے فرمایا قالون ! رومی زبان میں۔ اس کا ترجمہ ہے ٹھیک فتوی دیا ۔
If that [timeframe] accommodates it, it is permissible for the first husband to believe her if his dominant opinion is that she is truthful. The wife will marry and fifteen days pass. Then she will observe the 'iddah of the second husband for forty-nine days, so fifteen and forty-nine make sixty-four days. The 'iddah of the first husband is forty-nine days and the 'iddah of the second husband is fifty-four days. The total is ninety-three days. The diagram is as follows:

First husband's divorce and 'iddah: menstruation, purity, menstruation, purity, menstruation, total 39 days (15+15+9) -> actually the diagram shows 39 as total.
Second husband's marriage, divorce and 'iddah: purity, menstruation, purity, menstruation, purity, menstruation, total 54 days.
Sum of both 'iddahs: 39 + 54 = 93 days.

Therefore, if she returns after 93 days, she can be believed.

There is proof in an athar: From al-Sha'bi: A man came to Ali ibn Abi Talib and said, 'I divorced my wife, and she came after two months and said, "My 'iddah has ended."' Ali had Shurayh with him. He said, 'Say in this matter.' Shurayh said, 'And you are a witness, O Commander of the Faithful?' He said, 'Yes.' He said, 'If she brings trustworthy witnesses from her relatives who testify that she menstruated three times, [then she is truthful], otherwise she is a liar.' Ali said, 'Qaloon' in Roman language, meaning, 'I have hit the mark.' (Referenced in Sunan al-Bayhaqi, etc.) From this athar, it is understood that if the timeframe accommodates it and there is a dominant opinion that she is truthful, then her word will be accepted. The woman who is non-consummated has been given one divorce, so she only observes the 'iddah of the second husband, which will be at minimum 39 days. And she returns after two months, so it can be considered possible if circumstantial evidence indicates possibility; otherwise, generally, three months are needed for three menstrual cycles.

Footnote (a): Hadhrat Shu'bi said: A man came to Hadhrat Ali and said, 'I gave my wife three divorces, then she came after two months and said my 'iddah has ended.' Hadhrat Ali had Qadi Shurayh with him. He said, 'Qadi, what do you say?' Shurayh said, 'O Commander of the Faithful, are you a witness?' He said, 'Yes.' Shurayh said, 'If she brings trustworthy witnesses from her close family who testify that she had three menstruations, then she is truthful; otherwise she is a liar.' So Hadhrat Ali said, 'Qaloon!' in the Roman language. Its translation is, 'You gave a correct ruling.'
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Samari addresses the issue of a woman who claims her 'iddah has ended after two months, contrary to the typical three menstrual cycles. The author explains that if the time frame makes it possible (i.e., the minimum possible 'iddah could have been completed within two months), and there is a strong presumption that she is truthful, her claim may be accepted. The proof is an athar from Ali ibn Abi Talib. In the athar, Shurayh advises that she must bring trustworthy witnesses to confirm three menstruations; otherwise, she is lying. The author comments that for a non-consummated divorcee, the minimum 'iddah for the second husband is 39 days, so two months may accommodate it. The general principle is that if her claim is plausible and evidence supports it, it is accepted.

Key Terms
اثر (athar): A narration from a Companion (here, Ali ibn Abi Talib) used as proof; distinct from hadith (prophetic).
غالب ظن (ghalib al-zann): Dominant or overwhelming presumption; a degree of certainty that suffices for legal decisions when absolute certainty is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary condition for a woman's claim of 'iddah completion to be accepted according to the Hanafi ruling in this passage?
A) The timeframe must make it possible and the husband must have a dominant presumption of her truthfulness.
B) She must bring two male witnesses.
C) The divorce must have been pronounced three times.
D) She must have completed exactly three menstrual cycles.

Key Takeaway
A woman's claim of completed 'iddah is accepted in Hanafi fiqh if the timeframe possibly allows and the husband's dominant presumption supports her truthfulness.

Definition and Ruling of Ila' (Vow of Abstinence)
Opening of the chapter on Ila' with definition, Quranic proof, and initial ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ila' and state the ruling when the husband fulfills or breaks the oath within four months.

Core Idea
Ila' is an oath to abstain for four months; breaking it requires expiation, fulfilling it leads to divorce.

This lesson introduces the chapter on Ila' (vow of abstinence). Ila' is a husband's solemn oath to refrain from sexual relations with his wife for four months or more. The Quran establishes a four-month waiting period and outlines two outcomes: return (intercourse) with expiation, or divorce upon expiry. We will examine the definition, the primary proof from Surah al-Baqarah, and the immediate legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and its Ruling
First, understand what ila' is: an oath (yamin) by the husband to abstain from intercourse for a specified duration of four months or more. The ruling is that it is a valid oath with consequences.
The term 'mu'li' refers to the husband who makes such an oath.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The primary proof is Qur'an 2:226-227. Read the verse carefully and note its structure: it mentions a four-month waiting period, then two conditionals: 'if they return' (fa'u) and 'if they resolve on divorce'.
The verse is decisive in transmission (qat'i al-thubut) but its meaning requires interpretation (zanni al-dalalah).
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah (How the Proof Proves the Ruling)
The phrase 'waiting of four months' establishes the minimum duration for ila'. The conditional 'if they return' indicates that resuming relations within the period cancels the ila' and requires expiation (implied by 'Allah is Forgiving'). The conditional 'if they resolve on divorce' shows that divorce becomes effective after four months without intercourse.
The proof for expiation comes from the general rules of oath-breaking (hanath).
4
Note the Conditions and Consequences
From the matn, we learn: if the husband says 'By Allah, I will not approach you' (unspecified duration) or specifies 'four months', it is ila'. If he has intercourse within four months, he breaks his oath (hanath) and must give kaffarah (expiation). If he refrains for the full four months, a revocable divorce (talaq ba'in) takes effect.
If the oath is for less than four months, it is not legally ila' for divorce, but breaking it still requires expiation.
5
Evaluate the Proof's Strength
The Qur'anic verse is the pillar; the hadith (Bukhari) and athar from Ibn Abbas support the requirement of an oath and the four-month duration. The Hanafi position relies on these texts without istihsan override.
The ruling on expiation is wajib (obligatory) based on the general oath-breaking rule.

Chapter of Ila'
[1990] (1) When a man says to his wife, 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' or 'By Allah, I will not approach you for four months,' then he is a mu'li. [1991] (2) And if he has intercourse with her within the four months, he breaks his oath, and expiation is incumbent upon him, and the [status of] ila' is removed.

Necessary note: The meaning of ila' is an oath. In sacred law, taking an oath to not approach one's wife for four months is called ila'. If he takes an oath not to approach for four months and does not approach, then one irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) occurs. And if he does approach, he must give expiation for the oath. And if he takes an oath not to approach for less than four months, in common parlance this is also ila', but divorce does not occur due to it. However, if he approaches before that period, expiation for the oath becomes necessary; and if he does not approach by that time, no divorce occurs. Its proof is in this verse: 'For those who swear off their wives is a waiting of four months. Then if they return, indeed Allah is Forgiving, Merciful. And if they resolve on divorce, indeed Allah is Hearing, Knowing.' (Surah al-Baqarah 2:226-227). (a) This verse shows that it is ila' when there are four months. (2) In hadith, the Messenger of Allah performed ila' from his wives for one month. Anas ibn Malik heard: 'The Messenger of Allah performed ila' from his wives, and his foot was sprained; so he stayed in his upper chamber for twenty-nine days.' (b) (Sahih al-Bukhari, chapter on the verse of ila'.)
(1990) (1) When a man says to his wife, 'By Allah, I will not approach you,' or 'By Allah, I will not approach you for four months,' then he becomes a mu'li. In the first case, he did not specify a period, leaving it general, so it is forever; therefore it includes four months, so ila' is established. In the second case, he explicitly said four months, so according to the verse, the condition of four months is fulfilled; thus ila' is established. That ila' requires an oath is proven by this athar: From Ibn Abbas: 'There is no ila' except with an oath.' (c) (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). From this athar, it is understood that only when he says it with an oath does it become ila'. And the condition of four months is proven by the verse above.
Mu'li: It is the active participle from ila', meaning one who performs ila'.
[1991] (2) So if he has intercourse within the four months, he becomes a breaker of his oath (hanith), and expiation becomes necessary for him, and ila' becomes nullified. Because he took an oath not to go to his wife for four months, and he met his wife before that, so expiation for the oath becomes necessary.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on Ila' (vow of abstinence) from Al-Sharh al-Samari. Ila' is defined as an oath sworn by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period of four months or more. The legal ruling is based on Quran 2:226-227, which gives a four-month waiting period: if the husband returns (i.e., resumes relations) within that time, he is forgiven upon expiation; if he resolves on divorce, a divorce takes effect. The matn states two scenarios: if the husband says 'By Allah, I will not approach you' without specifying a duration, the oath is perpetual and thus includes four months, so it constitutes ila'. If he specifies four months, it is explicitly ila'. The commentator adds that if the oath is for less than four months, it is ila' in common terminology but does not trigger divorce; however, if broken, expiation is still required. The requirement of an oath is proven by a narration from Ibn Abbas: 'There is no ila' except with an oath.' The ruling on breaking the oath is that if intercourse occurs within the four months, the husband is hanith (oath-breaker) and must pay kaffarah (expiation), and the ila' is cancelled. If he does not have intercourse until the end of the four months, a revocable divorce (talaq ba'in) occurs upon the expiry.

Key Terms
ايلاء (ila'): An oath sworn by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period of four months or more.
حنث (hanath): The act of breaking an oath, which in the context of ila' occurs if the husband has intercourse with his wife within the specified four-month period.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath; in ila', it becomes wajib if the husband resumes relations before four months.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Definition and Conditions of Ila'
Defines ila' and provides textual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ila' and identify the conditions that make a valid ila'.

Core Idea
Ila' is a sworn abstinence of four months or more — a valid oath triggers legal consequences.

This lesson introduces the legal concept of ila' (vow of abstinence). The source text defines when a husband becomes a muli, the Quranic basis for the waiting period, and a Prophetic example. Understanding these fundamentals is essential for grasping the subsequent rules on the consequences of ila'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its definition
Read the opening sentence: when a man says to his wife 'By Allah, I will not approach you' (with or without a time limit), he becomes a muli. This is the act of ila'.
The oath is essential.
2
Note the conditions for a valid ila'
From the text, two conditions emerge: (1) the husband must swear an oath (e.g., by Allah), and (2) the period of abstinence must be four months or more (or indefinite, implying that duration).
The companion statement of Ibn Abbas confirms the need for an oath.
3
Locate the Quranic evidence
The verse 'For those who swear abstinence from their wives is a waiting period of four months…' (2:226) is cited directly. This establishes the waiting period and implicitly confirms that ila' triggers it.
This is the primary dalil.
4
Examine the Prophetic practice
The hadith of Anas ibn Malik shows that the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed ila' for 29 days. This demonstrates that ila' is valid even for less than four months, but the legal consequence of divorce only applies after four months.
The hadith does not change the minimum threshold for legal effect.
5
Summarize the Hanafi ruling
Combine the definition, conditions, and evidence: ila' is a valid oath to abstain from intercourse for four months or more, leading to a waiting period. The husband may return (fay') within that time to avoid divorce.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الايلاء. اذا قال الرجل لامرأته والله لا اقربک او لا اقربك اربعة اشهر فهي مول. للذين يؤلون من نسائهم تربص اربعة اشهر فان فاء وا فان الله غفور رحيم. سمع انس بن مالك يقول الى رسول الله من نسائه وكانت انفكت رجله فاقام في شربة له تسعا و عشرين. عن ابن عباس قال لا ايلاء الا بحلف.

For those who swear abstinence from their wives is a waiting period of four months. Then if they return, then Allah is Forgiving, Merciful. Anas ibn Malik said: The Messenger of Allah swore abstinence from his wives, and his foot was sprained, so he stayed in his chamber for twenty-nine nights. Ibn Abbas said: There is no ila' except with an oath.

Commentary
This passage defines the legal concept of ila' (vow of abstinence). The author first gives the matn: if a man says to his wife 'By Allah, I will not approach you' without specifying a duration, or 'I will not approach you for four months,' he becomes a muli (one who has made ila'). The condition is that the oath is sworn by Allah and the period is either unspecified (which defaults to more than four months) or explicitly four months. The Quranic proof is cited: 'For those who swear abstinence from their wives is a waiting period of four months; then if they return, Allah is Forgiving, Merciful' (2:226). This establishes the waiting period. The hadith of Anas shows that the Prophet himself practiced ila' for 29 days, which indicates that ila' is valid even for less than four months, though the legal consequences of divorce only apply after four months. The statement of Ibn Abbas clarifies that ila' requires an oath; without an oath, it is not considered ila'.

Key Terms
إيلاء (īlā'): A vow to abstain from sexual intercourse with one's wife for a period of four months or more.
مول (mūlī): The husband who makes the vow of ila'.

Effect of Intercourse During Ila' Period
Ruling statement with Quranic evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband has intercourse during the ila' period, and identify the required expiation and its evidence.

Core Idea
Intercourse within ila' period breaks the oath and requires kaffarah; ila' is void.

This passage explains what happens if a husband who has sworn an oath of abstinence (ila') breaks it by having intercourse within the four-month waiting period. The text states that he becomes an oath-breaker (hanith), must pay expiation (kaffarah), and the ila' contract is void. The ruling is grounded in Quranic verses and a companion report from Ibn Abbas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the act in question (intercourse within the four months) and the legal consequences: hanth, kaffarah, and termination of ila'.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the primary evidence: Quran 2:226 (reference to 'return' i.e., intercourse) and Quran 5:89 (general expiation for oaths), plus the Ibn Abbas report.
The verses are directly quoted in the passage.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the Quranic phrase 'فإن فاءوا' (then if they return) indicates that intercourse breaks the oath and requires expiation, as returning implies violation.
'Faa' is interpreted as returning to marital relations.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical outcome: the mufta bihi ruling is that kaffarah is wajib and ila' ends. No significant intra-Hanafi dispute is mentioned.
This is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان وطيها في الاربعة الاشهر حنث في يمينه ولزمته الكفارة وسقط کتاب الايلاء. ذلک کفارہ ایمانکم اذا حلفتم و احفظوا ایمانکم. للذين يؤلون من نسائهم تربص اربعة اشهر فان فاء وا فان الله غفور رحيم. عن ابن عباس في آية الايلاء قال الرجل يحلف لامرأته بالله لا ينكحها تتربص اربعة اشهر فان هو نكحها كفر عن يمينه باطعام عشرة مساكين او كسوتهم او تحرير رقبة فمن لم يجد فصيام ثلاثة ايام وان مضت اربعة اشهر قبل ان ينكحها خيره السلطان الخ
If he has intercourse with her within the four months, he breaks his oath, and the kaffarah becomes obligatory upon him, and the book of ila' is dropped. 'That is the expiation of your oaths when you swear, and guard your oaths' (5:89). 'For those who swear abstinence from their wives is a waiting period of four months; then if they return, then Allah is Forgiving, Merciful' (2:226). From Ibn Abbas regarding the verse of ila': the man swears by Allah that he will not have intercourse with his wife; he waits four months; if he has intercourse, he expiates his oath by feeding ten poor persons or clothing them or freeing a slave; if he cannot, then fasting three days; if four months pass before he has intercourse, the ruler gives him an option.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage explains the legal effect of the husband having intercourse with his wife within the four-month waiting period. The matn states that if he has intercourse, he becomes hanith (one who breaks his oath) and must pay kaffarah (expiation). The ila' is rendered void because the purpose of the waiting period is no longer applicable. The first Quranic verse cited (5:89) is the general verse on expiation of oaths, which requires feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave, with fasting as an alternative. The second verse (2:226) is the ila' verse itself, where 'return' (faa') means returning to marital relations, and this leads to forgiveness. The report from Ibn Abbas elaborates: if the husband has intercourse within four months, he expiates his oath; if he does not, the ruler gives him an ultimatum. This shows that the Hanafi ruling is based on the literal meaning of the Quran and the practice of the Companions.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): The state of breaking an oath; becoming an oath-breaker.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for breaking an oath, as prescribed in Quran 5:89: feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave; fasting if unable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Automatic Divorce After Ila' – Hanafi vs Shafi'i
States ruling on automatic divorce after four months of ila'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the automatic divorce after ila', identify the Shafi'i opposing view, and explain the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Four months of abstinence ends in automatic irrevocable divorce; no separate pronouncement needed.

The passage tackles the final stage of ila' (vow of abstinence). After the four-month waiting period, Hanafi law holds that one talaq bayyinah takes effect automatically. This view is grounded in the practice of Companions like Ibn Abbas, Uthman, and Zayd. The Shafi'i school requires the ruler to intervene, leading to a significant difference in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the automatic consequence of completing four months without intercourse: one irrevocable divorce.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school holds that after four months, the ruler gives an ultimatum; divorce does not occur automatically.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i use the Quranic phrase 'and if they resolve on divorce' to argue that an act of resolution (divorce pronouncement) is required.
Quran 2:227
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis rely on the literal meaning of the ila' verse (2:226) and the unanimous practice of the Companions, which indicates automatic divorce after the term.
Companion reports override any ambiguity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi – wife is divorced automatically after four months; Shafi'i – husband must still decide or be compelled by ruler.
Affects remarriage and iddah calculations.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان لم يقربها حتى مضت اربعة اشهر بانت بتطليقة واحدة. ذلک کفارہ ایمانکم اذا حلفتم و احفظوا ایمانکم. للذين يؤلون من نسائهم تربص اربعة اشهر فان فاء وا فان الله غفور رحيم. عن ابن عباس في آية الايلاء قال الرجل يحلف لامرأته بالله لا ينكحها تتربص اربعة اشهر فان هو نكحها كفر عن يمينه باطعام عشرة مساكين او كسوتهم او تحرير رقبة فمن لم يجد فصيام ثلاثة ايام وان مضت اربعة اشهر قبل ان ينكحها خيره السلطان الخ. قلت لسعيد بن جبير اكان ابن عباس يقول اذا مضت اربعة اشهر فهي واحدة بتائينة ولا عدة عليها وتزوج ان شاء ت قال نعم. عثمان و زيد بن ثابت كانا يقولان اذا مضت الاربعة اشهر فهي تطليقة بائنة.
And if he does not approach her until four months have passed, she becomes separated with one talaq. 'That is the expiation of your oaths...' 'For those who swear abstinence...' From Ibn Abbas: ...if four months pass before he has intercourse, the ruler gives him an option. I said to Sa'id ibn Jubayr: 'Did Ibn Abbas say that when four months pass, it is one irrevocable divorce and there is no iddah upon her, and she may marry if she wishes?' He said: 'Yes.' 'Uthman and Zayd ibn Thabit used to say: when four months pass, it is one irrevocable divorce.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage states the final consequence of ila': if the husband does not have intercourse during the four-month period, one talaq bayyinah (irrevocable divorce) takes effect automatically. The evidence includes the Quranic verses and the reports from Ibn Abbas, 'Uthman, and Zayd ibn Thabit. The report from Ibn Abbas explicitly says that after four months, she is divorced irrevocably and does not have to observe iddah (waiting period) from that divorce; she can remarry immediately. 'Uthman and Zayd ibn Thabit concur. The passage also mentions the Shafi'i view (in the Urdu commentary) that after four months, the ruler gives an ultimatum: either divorce or keep her. This is the opposing view. The Hanafi position is that the divorce occurs automatically upon the expiry of four months without any additional requirement.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce; the wife becomes completely separated and cannot be remarried without a new marriage contract after an intervening marriage.
تطليقة (taṭlīqah): One pronouncement of divorce.
عدة ('iddah): The waiting period after divorce before a woman may remarry. Here, Ibn Abbas says no iddah is required for this type of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens automatically when the four-month period of ila' expires without intercourse?
A) The husband must give a divorce
B) One talaq bayyinah occurs
C) The couple is separated by judicial decree
D) The oath is broken and kaffarah is due

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the completion of four months of ila' results in an automatic talaq bayyinah, a ruling based on the practice of the Companions.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
6 min
Rulings on Ila' (Vow of Abstinence)
Explains the duration and conditions of Ila' and its legal effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the duration of Ila' and apply the conditions to determine when a vow constitutes Ila'.

Core Idea
Ila' requires a minimum four-month oath; a perpetual oath continues through remarriages until three divorces.

This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri explains the legal definition and effects of Ila'. It clarifies the duration, conditions, and consequences of the husband's vow. Understanding these rules is essential for distinguishing valid Ila' from other oaths and for knowing when divorce occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is Ila'? When is it valid? The passage defines Ila' as a vow to abstain from intercourse for at least four months. If the oath is for less, it is not Ila'.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse 2:226 is cited: 'For those who swear abstinence from their wives, a waiting of four months.' This is the key evidence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit mention of 'arba'ati ashhur' (four months) establishes the minimum period. The Hanafi school takes this as a binding condition.
4
Examine the perpetual oath scenario
If the husband swears forever, the oath remains and Ila' returns after each remarriage until three divorces occur.
5
Note the conditions and exceptions
Oaths for less than four months are not Ila'. Conditional oaths (e.g., 'if I have intercourse, I must fast') are Ila' if the duration is four months or more.

حاشیہ : (الف) ابراہیم نے فرمایا اگر چار ماہ گزر جائے تو اس سے بائنہ ہوگی ۔ پس اگر اس کے بعد اس شوہر سے شادی کی تو ایلام کرنے والا ہوگا۔ اور اگر صحبت نہیں کی یہاں تک کہ چار ماہ گزر گئے تو بائنہ ہو جائے گی۔ اور اگر اس کے بعد شادی کی تو پھر ایلاء کرنے والا ہوگا۔ اور اگر صحبت نہیں کی یہاں تک کہ چار ماہ گزر گئے تو پھر پائندہ ہو جائے گی (ب) حضرت ابن عباس نے فرمایا زمانہ جاہلیت میں ایلاء ایک سال دو سال تک یا اس سے زیادہ ہوتا تھا۔ پس اللہ نے اس کے لئے چار ماہ متعین کر دیا۔ پس اگر ایلاء چار ماہ سے کم ہو تو وہ ایلا نہیں ہے۔
Book of Ila'. (4) If he swore for four months, the oath is dropped. (5) If he swore for perpetuity, the oath remains, and if he returns and marries her, Ila' returns; if he has intercourse, expiation is incumbent; otherwise, after four months, another divorce occurs. If he marries her a third time, Ila' returns and after four months another divorce occurs. There is an athar from Ibn Umar: When four months pass, he is stopped until he divorces, and divorce does not occur until he divorces. This is mentioned from Uthman, Ali, Abu Darda, Aisha, and twelve companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him). (Bukhari, Bab Qawl Allah Ta'ala...). From this athar it is understood that after four months, he is made to wait; either he takes her back or the husband divorces and separates. (1993) (4) So if he swore for four months, the oath becomes invalid. He had sworn not to go to his wife for four months; he completed that and one divorce occurred making her bain. So the oath is fulfilled. If he now marries her again and has intercourse, no divorce occurs and no expiation is required because the oath was only for four months and has been fulfilled. (1994) (5) If he swore for perpetuity, the oath remains. So if he returns and marries her, Ila' returns. If he has intercourse, expiation is incumbent; otherwise, after four months, a second divorce occurs. If he marries her a third time, Ila' returns and after four months a third divorce occurs. If he marries her after a second husband, no divorce occurs from this Ila', but the oath remains; if he has intercourse, he gives expiation for his oath. This issue is based on two principles: First, if he swore for perpetuity, whenever in his life he has intercourse with this wife, he must give expiation because the oath remains. Second, the Ila' is effective only before a second husband; after a second husband, when she returns, this Ila' is not effective, and after that, if he does not meet her for four months, no divorce occurs. Explanation: The husband swore never to meet her. If he meets her within four months, Ila' ends but expiation is incumbent. If he does not meet her, one bain divorce occurs. Then if he marries her again, Ila' is restored because the perpetual oath remains. If this time he meets her within four months, Ila' ends and expiation is incumbent; if not, a second divorce occurs after four months. Then if he marries her a third time, Ila' is restored. Hashiyah (a): Narrated from Ibn Umar: When four months pass, he is made to wait until he divorces; divorce does not occur until he divorces. This is narrated from Uthman, Ali, Abu Darda, Aisha, and ten companions. (Chapter II: Ila' continued) After four months, another divorce; if he marries her after a second husband, no divorce occurs from that Ila', and the oath remains; if he has intercourse, he gives expiation for his oath. (1995) (6) If he swore for less than four months, he is not a muli. The verse clearly states that the oath is for four months, so if he swore for less than four months, it is not Ila' from which a bain divorce occurs. (Surah Baqarah 2:226) Athar from Ibn Abbas: The Ila' of the Jahiliyyah was for a year, two years, or more; Allah set it as four months. So if his Ila' is less than four months, it is not Ila'. (1996) (7) If he swore by Hajj, or fasting, or charity, or freeing a slave, or divorce, then he is a muli. For example, if he says: If I go to my wife within four months, then Hajj is obligatory on me, or fasting, or charity, or my slave is free, or my wife is divorced, then in such a case Ila' is contracted. Hashiyah (a): Ibrahim said: When four months pass, she becomes bain from him. If he marries her after that, he is a muli. If he does not have intercourse until four months pass, she becomes bain. If he marries her after that, he is again a muli. And if he does not have intercourse until four months pass, she becomes bain again. (b) Ibn Abbas said: The Ila' of the Jahiliyyah was for a year, two years, or more; Allah set for them four months. So if Ila' is less than four months, it is not Ila'.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage from Al-Sharh al-Thamiri discusses the rulings of Ila' (vow of abstinence from marital relations). It is a ruling with proof type, drawing from Quranic verse 2:226 and hadith. The passage first addresses the duration of the oath: if a husband swears to abstain for exactly four months, the oath is fulfilled after four months resulting in one bain divorce, and no further obligation remains. If he swears for perpetuity (forever), the oath continues indefinitely. Upon remarriage after the first divorce, Ila' is renewed; if he again abstains for four months, another divorce occurs, up to three. After a third divorce, remarriage requires a lawful intermediary (halaal). If she marries another man and then returns to the first husband, the previous Ila' is not revived because a new marriage contract is established, though the perpetual oath still requires expiation for any future intercourse. The passage then explains that an oath for less than four months does not constitute Ila' because the Quran specifies a four-month period. Finally, it states that if the husband conditions his abstinence on acts like Hajj, fasting, charity, freeing a slave, or divorce, he is considered a muli (one who has taken Ila'), and the same rulings apply. The commentator emphasizes that the key is the intention to harm the wife by depriving her of marital intimacy. The Hanafi position is clear: the minimum period is four months, and the oath must be explicit to trigger Ila' rulings.

Key Terms
الايلاء (al-īlā'): A vow (yamin) by a husband to abstain from sexual intercourse with his wife for a period of four months or more.
مول (mūlī): The husband who has taken the vow of Ila'.
اليمين (al-yamīn): The oath taken by the husband to abstain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following oaths does NOT constitute Ila' according to the Hanafi school?
A) 'I will not have intercourse with you for six months.'
B) 'I will not have intercourse with you for three months.'
C) 'I will not have intercourse with you forever.'
D) 'If I have intercourse with you, I will fast one month.'

Key Takeaway
Ila' is a valid vow of abstinence that requires a minimum four-month oath; perpetual oaths continue until three divorces occur.

Minimum Duration for Ila': Four Months Required
Ruling with proof on the minimum duration for Ila'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum duration for Ila' and apply the Quranic and athar evidence to determine whether a given oath constitutes Ila'.

Core Idea
Ila' requires a four-month oath; less than that is not Ila'.

This passage clarifies a key condition for Ila' (a vow of abstention): the oath must be for four months or more. The ruling is derived directly from the Quran and further supported by the practice of the Companions. Understanding this minimum threshold is essential to correctly identify cases of Ila' and its legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is a husband swearing to abstain from intercourse. The ruling is that if the oath is for less than four months, he is not a Muli (one who performs Ila').
Ila' is a specific legal term; not every vow of abstention qualifies.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The Quranic verse (2:226) states a waiting period of four months for those who swear off their wives. This implies that Ila' is only recognized for oaths of four months or more.
The verse is qati al-thubut (certain transmission) and qati al-dalala (clear meaning) for the minimum duration.
3
Analyze the secondary proof
An athar from Ibn Abbas explains that pre-Islamic Ila' could be longer, but Islam limited it to four months. This confirms that oaths for less than four months are not Ila'.
The athar is a mawquf narration from a Companion, providing contextual understanding.
4
Understand the legal consequence
If the oath is not Ila', the husband does not face the four-month waiting period or the possibility of automatic divorce. The oath is simply a personal vow without marital legal effect.
Other schools may differ, but the Hanafi school strictly follows the Quranic limit.

If he swore for less than four months, he is not a Muli. [1995] (6) So if he took an oath for less than four months, he will not be a Muli. The verse specifies that an oath of four months is called Ila'. Therefore, if he swore for less than four months, it will not be Ila' from which an irrevocable divorce occurs. "For those who swear off their wives, a waiting period of four months" (Quran 2:226). (2) There is an athar: from Ibn Abbas, he said: The Ila' of the people of Jahiliyyah was for a year, two years, or more. Then Allah set for them a limit of four months. So if his Ila' is less than four months, it is not Ila'. (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, book: the man who swears not to approach his wife for less than four months, vol. 7, p. 625, no. 15237; Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, narration 18581) This athar shows that if he swore for less than four months, it is not Ila'.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the minimum duration for Ila'. The author states clearly that if a husband swears an oath to abstain from intercourse for less than four months, he is not considered a Muli (one who performs Ila'). The evidence is from the Quranic verse (2:226) which specifies a waiting period of four months for those who swear off their wives. Additionally, an athar from Ibn Abbas explains that the pre-Islamic custom of Ila' could be for one or two years or more, but Islam limited it to four months. If the oath is for less than that, it does not count as Ila'. The Urdu commentary reinforces this: such an oath does not lead to an irrevocable divorce.

Key Terms
إيلاء (īlā'): A vow by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period of four months or more.

Ila' by Vow of Hajj, Fasting, Charity, etc.
Ruling with proof on swearing by acts of worship for Ila'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on Ila' when the husband swears by acts of worship and identify the evidence from the athar of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
Any oath that prevents intercourse, even indirectly, constitutes Ila'.

This lesson expands the concept of Ila' beyond direct oaths of abstention. The Hanafi school holds that if a husband swears by acts like Hajj, fasting, or divorce that he will not approach his wife, the oath is considered Ila'. This is based on a general principle derived from the Companion Ibn Abbas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing by Hajj, fasting, charity, freeing a slave, or divorce with the condition of not approaching the wife for four months. The ruling: it constitutes Ila'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the athar from Ibn Abbas: 'كل يمين منعت جماعا فهى ايلاء' (Every oath that prevents intercourse is Ila'). This is narrated by al-Bayhaqi and Abd al-Razzaq.
The athar is a marfu' hadith in meaning, though technically mawquf.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'كل' (every) indicates generality. Any oath that prevents intercourse, regardless of wording, falls under Ila'. Swearing by Hajj makes intercourse burdensome, thus preventing it.
This includes indirect prevention through imposing heavy obligations.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require a direct oath of abstention. Hanafis include indirect oaths based on the general principle.
The ikhtilaf stems from the interpretation of 'prevention'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi because the dalil is general and no specific exclusion applies. The principle is authoritative for fatwa.
The later Hanafi masters upheld this expansive view.

And if he swore by Hajj, or by fasting, or by charity, or by freeing a slave, or by divorce, then he is a Muli. [1996] (7) If he swore by Hajj, or fasting, or charity, or freeing a slave, or divorce, then he is one who does Ila'. For example, he says: If I go to my wife within four months, then Hajj is obligatory upon me, or fasting is obligatory upon me, or charity is obligatory upon me, or my slave is free, or my wife is divorced. In such a case, Ila' takes effect. The principle is that every oath conditioned on an act that makes it difficult for the husband to approach his wife constitutes Ila'. In the mentioned case, if he goes to his wife within four months, then Hajj etc. becomes obligatory, or divorce occurs, which makes it difficult to approach her, so Ila' takes effect. (2) There is an athar: from Ibn Abbas, he said: Every oath that prevents intercourse is Ila'. And we also narrated from al-Sha'bi and al-Tahsi. Commentary
This passage expands the scope of Ila' to include oaths tied to acts of worship or divorce. The author rules that if a husband swears by Hajj, fasting, charity, freeing a slave, or divorce that he will not approach his wife for four months, he becomes a Muli. The reasoning is that such oaths make it difficult for him to approach his wife because doing so would incur a heavy obligation (like Hajj) or result in divorce. The principle is that any oath that effectively prevents intercourse constitutes Ila'. This is supported by the athar from Ibn Abbas that 'every oath that prevents intercourse is Ila'.'

Ila' from Revocably vs Irrevocably Divorced Women
Ruling with proof on Ila' from divorced women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of Ila' from a revocably divorced woman and distinguish it from an irrevocably divorced woman, citing the Quranic proof.

Core Idea
Ila' is only valid from a wife; a revocably divorced woman is a wife, but an irrevocably divorced woman is not.

In this lesson, we examine a specific ruling from Hanafi fiqh regarding Ila' (swearing to abstain from marital relations). The key distinction is whether the woman is still legally a wife. The author of our text clarifies that Ila' is valid from a revocably divorced woman during her iddah, but not from an irrevocably divorced woman. This ruling is derived directly from the Quranic verse that addresses 'their wives.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the two scenarios
The passage discusses Ila' from two types of divorced women: revocable (raj'iyya) and irrevocable (ba'in). Note the ruling for each.
2
Determine the legal status of each woman
A revocably divorced woman during her iddah is still considered a wife in every respect. An irrevocably divorced woman is no longer a wife. This status is the basis for the ruling.
The Hanafi position is that the iddah period of a revocable divorce maintains the marital bond.
3
Locate the scriptural proof
The author cites Quran 2:226: 'For those who swear off their wives (nisa'ihim)'. The word 'their wives' indicates that Ila' applies only to women who are legally wives.
The proof is from the Quran, which is qati al-thubut and qati al-dalala in this context.
4
Apply the proof to the two scenarios
Since the revocably divorced woman is still a wife, she falls under 'their wives' and Ila' is valid. The irrevocably divorced woman is not a wife, so she is excluded, and Ila' is invalid.
5
State the final ruling
Therefore, Ila' is valid from a revocably divorced woman (the husband is a Muli), and invalid from an irrevocably divorced woman (he is not a Muli).
This ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage differentiates between types of divorced women regarding Ila'. The author rules that if a husband performs Ila' from his wife who is in a revocable divorce (i.e., she is still in her iddah period and can be taken back), then he is considered a Muli. This is because during the iddah of a revocable divorce, she is still legally his wife. However, if he does Ila' from an irrevocably divorced woman (ba'in), he is not a Muli because she is no longer his wife. The proof is the Quranic verse (2:226) which uses the word 'their wives' (nisa'ihim), indicating that Ila' applies only to wives.

Key Terms
مطلقة رجعية (muṭallaqa raj'iyya): A woman who has been divorced with the right to return during the iddah period; she is still considered a wife.
مطلقة بائن (muṭallaqa bā'in): A woman who has been irrevocably divorced; the marriage is completely severed.

Duration of Ila' for a Slave Woman
Ruling with proof on the duration of Ila' for a slave woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the duration of Ila' for a slave woman in the Hanafi school and apply the rule to determine when Ila' takes effect.

Core Idea
A slave woman's Ila' lasts two months — half the time of a free woman's.

This lesson examines the specific ruling for Ila' (an oath of abstinence) when the wife is a slave. The Hanafi school differentiates between free and slave women, setting the waiting period at two months for the latter. We will explore the evidence from the athar of Hasan al-Basri and understand the legal consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is Ila' (an oath to abstain from marital relations) when the wife is a slave woman. The ruling: the duration is two months, after which if the husband does not return, a ba'in divorce occurs.
2
Locate the dalil
The evidence cited is an athar from Hasan al-Basri, recorded in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, stating that after two months without return, Ila' takes effect.
The athar is a mursal hadith (from a tabi'i), which is considered weak in thubut but accepted in Hanafi usul for non-crucial matters.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The explicit statement 'when two months have passed... then Ila' has taken effect' directly establishes the duration. The Hanafi school uses this to set the rule.
The dalala is zanni because it is a single athar.
4
Apply the ruling practically
If a husband swears to abstain from his slave wife for two months and does not return within that period, the Ila' becomes effective, resulting in a ba'in divorce. This is half of the free woman's four-month period.
The principle of half obligation for slaves is consistently applied in Hanafi fiqh.

(Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, narration 18605) This athar shows that the period of Ila' for a slave woman is two months.

This passage specifies the duration of Ila' for a slave woman. The author rules that if a husband swears to abstain from intercourse with his slave wife for two months, it constitutes Ila'. The evidence is an athar from Hasan al-Basri stating that after two months without returning, Ila' becomes effective. The Urdu commentary explains that the period is half of the free woman's four months, reflecting the general principle that the rights and obligations of slave women are often half of those of free women.

Verbal Retraction in Ila' When Unable to Consummate
Ruling on Ila' when intercourse is impossible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which verbal retraction suffices to nullify Ila' and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on this issue.

Core Idea
When intercourse is impossible, verbal retraction—not physical return—nullifies Ila'.

What happens when a husband makes a vow of abstinence (Ila') but then becomes unable to have intercourse due to illness or distance? Does the oath automatically lapse, or must he still physically return? This passage clarifies that in cases of genuine incapacity, the obligation of physical consummation is replaced by a simple verbal declaration, reflecting the Hanafi principle that obligations are lifted when impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the passage and find the final ruling: What action nullifies Ila' when the husband or wife has a valid excuse?
The ruling is that verbal retraction (saying 'I have returned to her') suffices.
2
List the excuses
Extract all the specific scenarios mentioned where physical intercourse is impossible: husband sick, wife sick, blocked passage, young age, distance, etc.
These are the conditions that trigger the alternative ruling.
3
Identify the method of retraction
Note the two methods mentioned: by tongue (verbal) and, according to Abu Hanifa, by heart (intention).
This reveals an intra-Hanafi difference.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare Abu Hanifa's view (heart intention suffices) with the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (verbal statement required).
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is verbal retraction.
5
Derive the practical rule
Synthesize the ruling: In all cases where intercourse is impossible, the husband need only verbally declare his return to nullify Ila'.
If he later becomes able, physical return is not required retroactively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كان المولى مريضا لا يقدر على الجماع او كانت المرأة مريضة او كانت رتقاء او صغيرة لا يجامع مثلها او بزرخها كانت عاجلة او كانت بينهما مسافة لا يقدر على الوصول اليها في مدة الايلاء فان رجع بلسانه او بقلبه عند ابى حنيفة رحمه الله يسقط الايلاء. [۱۹۹۹ (۱۰) اگر ایلاء کرنے والا بیمار ہو جس کی وجہ سے جماع پر قدرت نہ رکھتا ہو۔ یا عورت بیمار ہو یا بند راستہ والی ہو یا اتنی چھوٹی ہو کہ اس سے وطی نہ ہو سکتی ہو یا دونوں کے درمیان اتنی مسافت ہو کہ ایلاء کی مدت میں وہاں تک پہنچنے کی قدرت نہ ہو تو اس کا رجوع یہ کہہ دینا ہے کہ میں اس کی طرف رجوع کر لیا۔ جب یہ کہہ دے تو ایلا ء ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
And if the Muli is sick and unable to have intercourse, or the woman is sick, or she has a blocked passage, or she is too young to be penetrated, or she has a discharge, or there is a distance between them such that he cannot reach her within the period of Ila', then if he returns by his tongue or by his heart according to Abu Hanifa may Allah have mercy on him, the Ila' drops. [1999] (10) If the one who does Ila' is sick such that he cannot have intercourse, or the woman is sick, or she has a blocked passage, or she is so young that intercourse is not possible, or there is a distance such that he cannot reach her within the period of Ila', then his return is to say: 'I have returned to her.' When he says this, the Ila' drops.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage addresses situations where actual intercourse is impossible due to a valid excuse. The author rules that if the husband or wife has a legitimate reason preventing intercourse (e.g., illness, physical blockage, extreme distance), then the requirement to physically consummate the return is waived. Instead, verbal retraction (saying 'I have returned to her') is sufficient to nullify the Ila'. According to Abu Hanifa, even intending in the heart suffices, but the relied-upon view is verbal. The ruling is based on the principle that obligations are lifted in cases of incapacity.

Key Terms
رجوع (rujū'): The act of returning to marital relations, either physically or verbally, to nullify Ila'.
إيلاء (īlā'): A vow made by a husband to abstain from sexual relations with his wife for a period of four months or more.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what suffices to nullify Ila' when the husband is sick and cannot have intercourse?
A) Physical intercourse as soon as he recovers
B) Verbal retraction (saying 'I have returned to her')
C) Paying a penalty (kaffarah)
D) Divorcing the wife

Key Takeaway
When physical return is impossible due to illness or other valid excuses, verbal retraction alone nullifies Ila' and prevents divorce.

The General Rule of Ila': Any Oath Preventing Intercourse
Establishes the general principle of ila' with supporting athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of ila' and identify the basis for its broad scope, including the key hadith.

Core Idea
Any oath that prevents marital intercourse is ila' — the defining element is the prevention, not the wording.

Ila' is a vow of abstinence that has serious legal consequences if the husband fails to resume intercourse within four months. The Hanafi school takes a comprehensive view: any oath, direct or indirect, that blocks marital intimacy is considered ila'. This lesson unpacks the general rule and the pivotal evidence from Ibn Abbas that establishes this broad definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? The act is a husband's oath that prevents marital intercourse. The ruling is that such an oath constitutes ila'.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites an athar (report) from Ibn Abbas: 'Every oath that prevents intercourse is ila'.' This is found in Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq and Sunan al-Bayhaqi.
The dalil is ahad (transmitted by few), but its meaning is clear.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase 'every oath' (كل يمين) is general and includes all oaths that result in prevention of intercourse, regardless of the specific wording or context.
Hanafi usul: al-amm yufidu al-wujub – general commands indicate obligation.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i school restricts ila' to oaths specifically about abstaining from intercourse, not indirect oaths. The Hanafi school includes all preventative oaths.
This difference stems from how the generality of the hadith is interpreted.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) because it is based on the apparent meaning of the hadith and supported by reports from al-Sha'bi and al-Tahsawi.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah confirms this as the correct position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ ہر وہ کام کی شرط پر قسم جس سے بیوی شوہر کا ملنا دشوار ہو جائے اس سے ایلاء کا انعقاد ہوتا ہے۔ صورت مذکورہ میں چار مہینے کے اندر اندر بیوی سے ملے گا تو حج وغیرہ لازم ہوگا یا بیوی کو طلاق واقع ہوگی جن کی وجہ سے بیوی سے ملنا دشوار ہو گیا اس لئے ایلاء ہو جائے گا (۲) اثر میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس قال كل يمين منعت جماعا فهى ايلاء وروينا ايضا عن الشعبي والتحصي (الف) (سن للبیہقی ، باب کل یمین منعت الجمع بكل حال اکثر من اربعة اشهر بان بحث الحالف فھی ایلاء ج سابع میں ۶۲۶ نمبر ۱۵۲۳۹ر مصنف عبدالرزاق باب ما حال بینه و بین امراته فهو ایلاء ج سادس ص ۴۴۸ نمبر ۱۱۶۱۱) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ ہر وہ قسم جو صحبت روک دے اس سے ایلاء منعقد ہوگا۔
The principle is that any oath conditioned on an act that makes the wife's meeting difficult for the husband constitutes ila'. In the mentioned case, if he meets his wife within four months, then Hajj etc. becomes obligatory, or divorce occurs for the wife, due to which meeting the wife becomes difficult, so ila' takes effect. (2) There is an impact: from Ibn Abbas, he said: 'Every oath that prevents intercourse is ila'.' And we also narrated from al-Sha'bi and al-Tahsawi. (Al-Bayhaqi, Bab kull yamin mana'at al-jima'... Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq...) From this impact, it became known that every oath that prevents intimacy establishes ila'.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage defines the general rule for ila' (a vow of abstinence). The author explains that ila' is constituted by any oath that makes marital intercourse difficult or impossible. The proof is from the impact of Ibn Abbas, which states directly that any oath preventing intercourse is ila'. This is a broad principle that covers not only explicit vows of abstinence but also oaths that indirectly hinder intercourse, such as swearing to perform Hajj or that divorce will occur. The inclusion of reports from al-Sha'bi and al-Tahsawi strengthens the authority. The Hanafi position takes this expansive view, unlike some other schools that restrict ila' to specific wordings.

Key Terms
إيلاء (īlā’): A vow by a husband to abstain from marital intercourse with his wife for a specified period, typically four months.
يمين (yamīn): An oath sworn by Allah, by which a person binds himself to do or refrain from an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what constitutes an ila'?
A) Only an oath specifically about abstaining from intercourse
B) Any oath that prevents marital intercourse, regardless of wording
C) An oath to perform Hajj that delays intercourse
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, ila' is defined broadly: any oath that prevents marital intercourse, regardless of its specific wording, triggers the rules of ila'.

Ila' from Raj'iyya vs Ba'ina Divorce
Distinguishes ila' based on type of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of ila' from a raj'iyya versus a ba'ina divorced woman and identify the Quranic proof that establishes this distinction.

Core Idea
Ila' is only valid from a woman who is legally a wife; a ba'ina divorced woman is not.

This lesson clarifies a key condition for the validity of ila' (a vow to abstain from marital relations). The ruling hinges on the legal status of the woman at the time of the vow. Understanding the distinction between revocable (raj'iyya) and irrevocable (ba'ina) divorce is essential for correctly applying this chapter of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of divorced women
First, recall the Hanafi classification of divorce: raj'iyya (first/second divorce, revocable during iddah) and ba'ina (third divorce or khul', irrevocable).
A raj'iyya divorced woman remains a wife in many legal aspects during her iddah.
2
State the ruling for each type
The matn rules explicitly: ila' from a raj'iyya divorced woman is valid; ila' from a ba'ina divorced woman is invalid.
3
Understand the underlying reason
The validity depends on whether the woman is legally considered a wife. A raj'iyya woman is still a wife; a ba'ina woman is not.
4
Examine the Quranic proof
The verse 'For those who swear off from their wives' (2:226) uses the word 'from their wives' (min nisa'ihim), restricting ila' to wives.
5
Apply the proof to the two cases
Since a raj'iyya woman is a wife, she falls under the verse. A ba'ina woman is not a wife, so she is excluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۹۹۷) (۸) اگر مطلقہ رجعیہ سے ایلاء کیا تو ایلاء کرنے والا ہوگا۔ اور اگر بائنہ سے ایلاء کیا تو ایلاء کرنے والا نہیں ہوگا۔ مطلقہ رجعیہ عدت کے اندر ہر اعتبار سے بیوی ہے اس لئے اس سے ایلاء ہوگا۔ اور مطلقہ بائنہ اب بیوی نہیں رہی اس لئے اس سے ایلاء نہیں ہو گا (۲) آیت میں ہے کہ بیوی سے ایلاء ہوگا اگر بیوی نہ ہو ایلام نہیں ہوگا ۔ للذين يؤلون من نسائهم ( آیت ٢٢٦ سورة البقرة ٢) اس آیت میں نسا کھم سے پتہ چلا کہ بیوی ہوتو ایلاء ہوگا ورنہ نہیں۔
(1997) (8) If he performs ila' from a raj'iyya divorced woman, he is a valid muli. And if he performs ila' from a ba'ina divorced woman, he is not a muli. A raj'iyya divorced woman is a wife in every respect during her iddah, therefore ila' from her is valid. A ba'ina divorced woman is no longer a wife, therefore ila' from her is invalid. (2) The verse says ila' from wives: 'For those who swear off from their wives' (2:226). This verse shows that ila' occurs only if she is a wife, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This ruling addresses the validity of ila' depending on the nature of previous divorce. The key distinction is between a raj'iyya (revocable) divorce and a ba'ina (irrevocable) divorce. A woman in the iddah of a raj'iyya divorce remains legally a wife in many aspects, including the right to inheritance and the possibility of reconciliation without a new marriage contract. Therefore, ila' from her is valid. In contrast, a ba'ina divorce severs the marital bond completely; the woman is no longer a wife, so ila' does not apply. The proof is derived from the Quranic verse 'for those who swear off from their wives' (2:226), which restricts ila' to wives. This is a clear ruling with no intra-Hanafi dispute.

Key Terms
مطلقة رجعية (muṭallaqa rajʿiyya): A woman divorced with a first or second divorce that is revocable; she remains a wife during her iddah.
مطلقة بائن (muṭallaqa bāʾina): A woman divorced irrevocably, either by a third divorce or a khul' divorce; she is no longer a wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
From which of the following women is ila' valid according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) A ba'ina divorced woman
B) A raj'iyya divorced woman during her iddah
C) A woman after iddah of a raj'iyya divorce
D) A woman who has been divorced thrice

Key Takeaway
Ila' is only valid when the woman is legally a wife; a raj'iyya divorced woman qualifies, a ba'ina does not.

Fi'a by Tongue When Intercourse Is Impossible
Ruling on fi'a (return) when unable to consummate due to valid excuse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on fi'a when intercourse is impossible and identify valid excuses that permit verbal declaration.

Core Idea
When intercourse is impossible due to a valid excuse, verbal declaration suffices for fi'a.

This lesson explores a specific exception in the law of ila' (vow of abstinence). The primary method of return (fi'a) is intercourse, but the Hanafi school recognizes that this may be physically or circumstantially impossible. Drawing on the athar of Ibn Mas'ud, the ruling allows a verbal substitute. You will learn which excuses qualify and how the school restricts the substitute to spoken words.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Fi'a (return) is wajib in order to remove the ila' and avoid automatic talaq. The primary method is intercourse.
This is the baseline rule.
2
Locate the dalil and its authority
The proof is the athar of Ibn Mas'ud, a hadith ahad recorded in early compilations. The text explicitly allows 'heart or tongue' when there is an excuse.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut but qati' al-dalalah on the permissibility.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar's operative phrase 'with his heart or tongue' shows that verbal declaration is a valid substitute. The Hanafi school restricts it to the tongue because the heart's intention is not a public legal act.
This is a reasoned extension (ijtihad) from the text.
4
Note the ikhtilaf and the mufta bihi
Ibn Mas'ud allowed heart as well. The Hanafi school specifies tongue. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that spoken words are required.
The thamarah al-khilaf: silent intention does not suffice in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۱۹۹۹ (۱۰) اگر ایلاء کرنے والا بیمار ہو جس کی وجہ سے جماع پر قدرت نہ رکھتا ہو۔ یا عورت بیمار ہو یا بند راستہ والی ہو یا اتنی چھوٹی ہو کہ اس سے وطی نہ ہو سکتی ہو یا دونوں کے درمیان اتنی مسافت ہو کہ ایلاء کی مدت میں وہاں تک پہنچنے کی قدرت نہ ہو تو اس کا رجوع یہ کہہ دینا ہے کہ میں اس کی طرف رجوع کر لیا۔ جب یہ کہہ دے تو ایلا ء ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
یہ مسئلے اس قاعدے پر ہیں کہ کسی مجبوری کی وجہ سے عورت سے جماع پر قدرت نہیں ہے تو پھر جماع کرنا ضروری نہیں ہوگا ۔ بلکہ زبان
حاشیہ : (الف) حضرت ابن عباس نے فرمایا ہر وہ قسم جو جماع کو روک دے تو وہ ایلاء ہے۔ حضرت شعی سے بھی یہی منقول ہے (الف) حضرت حسن باندی کے ایلاء کے بارے میں فرماتے تھے اگر دو ماہ گزر جائے اور شوہر رجوع نہ کرے تو ایلاء واقع ہو جائے گا۔

الشرح الثميرى الجزء الثالث)
۱۴۱
كتاب الايلاء
كانت بينهما مسافة لا يقدر ان يصل اليها فى مدة الايلاء ففيئه ان يقول بلسانه فئت اليها فان قال ذلك سقط الايلاء [ ۱۱۲۰۰۰ وان صح في المدة بطل ذلك الفيء وصار فيئه الجماع ۲۰۰۱ (۱۲) واذا قال لامرأته انت على حرام سئل عن نيته فان قال اردت [ ا سے کہہ دے کہ میں نے بیوی سے رجوع کر لیا تو اس کہہ دینے سے ایلاء ساقط ہو جائے گا اور قسم کا کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ البتہ اگر جماع پر قادر ہو تو جماع کرنے سے ہی ایلاء ساقط ہوگا۔
اس اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ وقال ابن مسعود فان كان به علة من كبر او مرض او حبس يحول بينه وبين الجماع فان فيشه ان يفيء بقلبه او لسانه (الف) (مصنف ابن ابی هیته ۲۹ امن قال لافی الہ الا الجماع ج رابع ص ۱۳۶، نمبر ۱۸۶۰۲ اسفن للبیہقی ، باب الفیة الجماع الا من عذرج سابع ص ۶۲۴ نمبر ۱۵۲۳۵ مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب الفی الجماع ج سادس ص ۴۶۲ نمبر ۱۱۶۷۷)
اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ عذر ہو تو زبان سے رجوع کر لینا بھی کافی ہو جائے گا اور ایلاء ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
ارتقاء : وہ عورت جس کا رتم ہڈی وغیرہ کی وجہ سے بند ہو، فیء : ایلاء سے رجوع کرنے کوفی کہتے ہیں۔
[1999 (10) If the one making ila' is sick such that he is unable to have intercourse, or the woman is sick, or she is a ratta' (blocked vagina), or so young that intercourse is not possible, or there is such a distance between them that he cannot reach her within the ila' period, then his return (fi'a) is to say with his tongue: 'I have returned to her.' When he says that, the ila' drops. This issue is based on the principle that due to an inability to have intercourse because of an excuse, intercourse is not necessary; rather, the tongue (verbal declaration suffices). Marginal note: (A) Ibn Abbas said: every oath that prevents intercourse is ila'. Also narrated from al-Sha'bi. (A) Hasan used to say about ila' from a slave woman: if two months pass and the husband does not return, ila' occurs. ... If there is a distance such that he cannot reach her within the ila' period, then his fi'a is to say with his tongue: 'I have returned to her.' If he says that, ila' drops. And if he becomes healthy within the period, that fi'a becomes invalid, and his fi'a must be actual intercourse. 2001 (12) And if he says to his wife 'You are haram to me', he is asked about his intention. If he said 'I intended lying', then it is so. ... There is evidence in this athar: Ibn Mas'ud said: if there is an excuse such as old age, sickness, or imprisonment that prevents intercourse, then his fi'a is to return with his heart or tongue. From this athar, it is known that if there is an excuse, verbal return is sufficient and ila' drops. Ratta': a woman whose vaginal passage is blocked due to bone etc. Fi'a: return from ila'.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage handles exceptional circumstances where actual intercourse is impossible. The Hanafi rule is that the primary way to return (fi'a) from ila' is through intercourse, but if there is a valid excuse—such as sickness of either spouse, the wife being a ratta' (with a blocked passage), extreme youth, excessive distance, or imprisonment—then a verbal declaration ('I have returned to her') suffices. This is based on the athar from Ibn Mas'ud, which explicitly allows heart or tongue as a substitute. The ruling follows the principle of necessity (darura): when the original obligation cannot be fulfilled, a substitute is accepted. Importantly, if the excuse ceases within the ila' period, the verbal fi'a becomes void and the husband must perform actual intercourse. The text also includes a marginal note (hashiya) clarifying the terms 'ratta' and 'fi'a'.

Key Terms
فيء (fay’): Return from ila'; the act by which the husband annuls his vow of abstinence.
رتقاء (ratqā’): A woman with a blocked or sealed vagina, preventing intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following situations can fi'a be made by verbal declaration alone?
A) The husband is temporarily tired
B) The wife is a ratta'
C) The wife is menstruating
D) The husband prefers not to have intercourse

Key Takeaway
Verbal fi'a is a valid substitute for intercourse only when a genuine physical or circumstantial barrier prevents consummation.
